Created Expressions as Indicators of the Eternal Speech
The author explains the relationship between the eternal attribute of speech and its created expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the recited Quran consists of created, divisible expressions that indicate the uncreated, indivisible attribute of God's speech.

Core Idea
The Quran's words are created; the attribute they indicate is uncreated and indivisible.

Having defined God's speech as an eternal attribute, the author now turns to the words we actually recite. He explains that these words—whether Arabic, Hebrew, or Syriac—are created letters and sounds. They serve as indicators (dalalat) of the eternal attribute. The author uses the analogy of the divine name 'Allah' to illustrate how a created, divisible word refers to an indivisible essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
The passage distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the created expressions that convey it.
This is the core of the Maturidi position.
2
Analyze the analogy with the name 'Allah'
Just as the word 'Allah' has parts (hamza, lam, ha) but refers to the one indivisible essence, so the recited Quran has parts but refers to the one indivisible attribute.
3
Understand the term 'Quran'
The author says the created expressions are called Quran because they convey God's speech.
The name 'Quran' applies to the created words, not the eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصوير مسألة الكلام ثم نشتغل بتصوير المسألة بيننا وبين الخصوم في القرآن، ليتبين بذلك اندفاع أكثر أسئلتهم التي يموهون بها على الضعفة فنقول : إن كلام الله تعالى صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ليست من جنس الحروف والأصوات، وهو صفة واحدة وهو أمر بما أمر به نهي عما نهى عنه، خبر عما أخبر عنه، وهو صفة أزلية، ثم هذه العبارات العربية أو العبرية أو السورية، عبارات عنه وهو يتأدى بها، وهذه العبارات حروف وأصوات وهي مخلوقة في محلها دلالات على الكلام الذي هو الصفة الأزلية الله تعالى، لها أبعاض وأجزاء، وأنصاف وأعشار وغير ذلك، وهي في أنفسها مختلفة، وكلام الله تعالى واحد ليس بمتجزئ ولا بمختلف، ولا بعربي ولا بعبري ولا بسرياني كما أن ذات الله تعالى واحد ويسمى بتسميات كثيرة وبألسنة مختلفة، وتلك الألفاظ التي هي تسميات له متبعضة متجزئة، وذاته تعالى عن كل ذلك منزه، لا يوصف بالتعدد و بالاختلاف ولا بالتجزئ والتبعض، فإذا قيل : الله تعالى. فهذه التسمية عربية، وله بكل لسان تسمية، ثم قولنا : الله، حروف وأصوات، وهي مخلوقة والهمزة منها ربعها وهي مع إحدى اللامين نصفها وهي مع اللامين ثلاثة أرباعها وهي مع الهاء كلها. فكذا الكلام الذي هو صفة الله تعالى مع هذه الألفاظ الدالة عليه، والعبارات التي يتأدى هو بها هذا سبيله، وهذه الألفاظ تسمى قرآنا، وكلام الله، لتأدي كلام الله تعالى بها، وهي في أنفسها مخلوقة، والكلام الذي هو صفة الله تعالى ليس بمخلوق. ومشايخنا من أئمة سمرقند الذين جمعوا بين علم الأصول والفروع، عباراتهم في هذا أن يقولوا: القرآن كلام الله تعالى وصفته وكلام الله غير مخلوق وكذا صفته، ولا يقولون على الإطلاق أن القرآن غير مخلوق، لئلا يسبق إلى
Portraying the Issue of Speech. Then we engage in portraying the issue between us and the opponents regarding the Quran, so that by this it becomes clear that most of their questions with which they deceive the weak are repelled. So we say: Indeed the speech of Allah Most High is an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah Most High, not of the genus of letters and sounds. It is one attribute: it is a command for what He commanded, a prohibition for what He prohibited, and an information for what He informed. It is an eternal attribute. Then these Arabic, Hebrew, or Syriac expressions are expressions for it, and it is conveyed through them. These expressions are letters and sounds, created in their locus, as indicators of the speech which is the eternal attribute of Allah Most High. They have parts, portions, halves, tenths, and other divisions, and they differ in themselves. But the speech of Allah Most High is one, not divisible, not differing, not Arabic, not Hebrew, not Syriac. Just as the essence of Allah Most High is one and is called by many names and in different languages, and those words which are names for Him are divided and partitioned, while His essence is transcendent above all that, not described with multiplicity, difference, division, or partition. For example, when we say 'Allah Most High', this name is Arabic, and He has a name in every language. Then our saying 'Allah' consists of letters and sounds, and they are created; the hamza is a quarter of it, together with one lam it is half, together with both lams it is three-quarters, and together with the ha' it is all of it. So similarly is the speech which is the attribute of Allah Most High in relation to these words indicating it and the expressions through which it is conveyed. These words are called Quran and speech of Allah because they convey the speech of Allah Most High, and in themselves they are created, while the speech which is the attribute of Allah Most High is not created. And our scholars from the imams of Samarkand who combined knowledge of principles and branches, their expressions in this are that they say: The Quran is the speech of Allah Most High and His attribute, and the speech of Allah is uncreated, and likewise His attribute. But they do not say absolutely that the Quran is uncreated, lest it be understood that...
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a precise formulation (taswir) of the theological issue of the Quran's nature. The author first states the purpose: to refute the opponents' deceptive questions. He then defines God's attribute of speech as an eternal, undivided attribute that is not composed of letters or sounds; it is one, encompassing command, prohibition, and information. The expressions in various languages (Arabic, Hebrew, Syriac) are created letters and sounds that serve as indicators of this eternal attribute. These expressions have parts and differences, while God's speech is free from division and variation. The author draws an analogy with the divine name 'Allah': the word 'Allah' is a created Arabic word with parts (hamza, lam, ha'), but the Essence it denotes is one and indivisible. Similarly, the recited Quran is created, while the attribute of speech it indicates is uncreated. Finally, he explains the cautious position of the Samarkand scholars: they affirm that the Quran is God's speech and attribute, and that God's speech is uncreated, but they avoid saying 'the Quran is uncreated' absolutely, to prevent misunderstanding that the recited words themselves are eternal.

حروف وأصوات
مخلوق
دلالات
قرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Quran (the recited words) is itself uncreated because it is God's speech.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the expressions are created and only indicate the eternal attribute; the attribute itself is uncreated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the expressions (عبارات) that convey God's speech?
A) They are eternal like the attribute
B) They are created and serve as indicators
C) They are uncreated because they are from God
D) They are partially created and partially eternal

Key Takeaway
The recited Quran is created; the attribute of speech it indicates is uncreated and indivisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How can you train your heart to move past the created expressions to the uncreated speech they point to? Commit today to reciting one verse with full presence of mind, consciously distinguishing between the letters and what they signify.
?
The author says the attribute of speech is not divisible. Yet your understanding of God’s speech may be fragmented by your own biases, limited knowledge, or selective focus. Which part of the Quran do you tend to emphasize while neglecting other parts? Will you read a passage this week that challenges your usual perspective, asking God to show you the unity of His speech despite your limited perception?
?
The analogy of the name 'Allah' shows that the word is created and divisible, but the essence is one. Do you ever reduce your concept of God to the names and descriptions you know, as if He is contained by them? Spend a moment in silent reflection, letting go of every word and concept, and simply be aware of the uncreated reality behind all expressions. What fear or attachment arises when you try to do this?

God's Eternal Speech and Created Expressions
Nasafi defines God's speech as an eternal attribute and distinguishes it from created expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's eternal attribute of speech and the created expressions that indicate it, and why the Quran is considered created in its physical form but uncreated in its essence.

Core Idea
God's speech is one eternal attribute; its expressions are created.

Nasafi begins by establishing the fundamental distinction between God's speech as an eternal attribute and the various expressions in human languages that convey it. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of the Quran and avoiding the error of equating the created letters and sounds with the uncreated attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very precise theological vocabulary.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two main categories: the eternal attribute of speech and the created expressions. Find how Nasafi describes each and the relationship between them.
The attribute is one, eternal, not composed of letters; expressions are multiple, created, composed of letters.
3
Analyze the analogy
Nasafi draws an analogy with the names of Allah. How does this analogy help clarify the distinction? Then conclude with the status of the Quran.
The Quranic expressions are created, but they convey the uncreated attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كلام الله تعالى صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ليست من جنس الحروف والأصوات، وهو صفة واحدة وهو أمر بما أمر به نهي عما نهى عنه، خبر عما أخبر عنه، وهو صفة أزلية، ثم هذه العبارات العربية أو العبرية أو السورية (١) ، عبارات عنه وهو يتأدى بها، وهذه العبارات حروف وأصوات وهي مخلوقة (٢) في محلها دلالات على الكلام الذي هو الصفة الأزلية الله تعالى، لها أبعاض وأجزاء، وأنصاف وأعشار وغير ذلك، وهي في أنفسها مختلفة، وكلام الله تعالى واحد ليس بمتجزئ ولا بمختلف، ولا بعربي ولا بعبري ولا بسرياني كما أن ذات الله تعالى واحد ويسمى بتسميات كثيرة وبألسنة مختلفة، وتلك الألفاظ التي هي لا تسميات له متبعضة متجزئة، وذاته تعالى عن كل ذلك منزه، لا يوصف بالتعدد و بالاختلاف ولا بالتجزئ والتبعض، فإذا قيل : الله تعالى. فهذه التسمية عربية، وله بكل لسان تسمية، ثم قولنا : الله، حروف وأصوات، وهي مخلوقة والهمزة منها ربعها وهي مع إحدى اللامين نصفها وهي مع اللامين ثلاثة أرباعها وهي مع الهاء كلها. فكذا الكلام الذي هو صفة الله تعالى مع هذه الألفاظ الدالة عليه، والعبارات التي يتأدى هو بها هذا سبيله، وهذه الألفاظ تسمى قرآنا، وكلام الله، لتأدي كلام الله تعالى بها، وهي في أنفسها مخلوقة، والكلام الذي هو صفة الله تعالى ليس بمخلوق
Indeed the speech of Allah the Exalted is an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted, not of the genus of letters and sounds, and it is one attribute, and it is commanding what He commanded, prohibiting what He prohibited, informing of what He informed, and it is an eternal attribute. Then these Arabic, Hebrew, or Syriac expressions are expressions for it, and it is conveyed through them, and these expressions are letters and sounds, and they are created in their place, indications of the speech which is the eternal attribute of Allah the Exalted; they have parts, divisions, halves, tenths, and other than that, and they are in themselves different. And the speech of Allah the Exalted is one, not divisible, not different, not Arabic, not Hebrew, not Syriac, just as the essence of Allah the Exalted is one and is called by many names and in different tongues. And those expressions which are not names for Him are divided and partitioned, and His essence is transcendent above all that, not described with multiplicity, difference, division, or partition. So when it is said: 'Allah the Exalted', this name is Arabic, and He has a name in every tongue. Then our saying: 'Allah' is letters and sounds, and they are created; the hamza of it is its quarter, and with one lam it is half, and with two lams it is three quarters, and with the ha it is all. So likewise the speech which is the attribute of Allah the Exalted with these expressions indicating it, and the expressions by which it is conveyed, have this status. And these expressions are called Quran and speech of Allah, because they convey the speech of Allah the Exalted, and they in themselves are created, and the speech which is the attribute of Allah the Exalted is not created.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of God's speech as an eternal attribute. Nasafi begins by stating that God's speech is an attribute that subsists in God's essence, not composed of letters and sounds, and it is one attribute that encompasses commanding, prohibiting, and informing. It is eternal. Then he distinguishes the attribute from the actual expressions in Arabic, Hebrew, or Syriac: these expressions are created letters and sounds that indicate the eternal attribute. They have parts and are different, while the attribute is one and indivisible. He uses an analogy: just as God's essence is one but called by different names in different languages, and those names are created and have parts (e.g., the word 'Allah' has letters and sounds), so too the expressions of the Quran are created and indicate the uncreated attribute. He concludes that these expressions are called Quran and speech of God because they convey the attribute, but they themselves are created, while the attribute is uncreated.

B) They are created and indicate the eternal attribute.
C) They are part of God's essence.
D) They are neither created nor eternal.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is an eternal, indivisible attribute; the letters and sounds of the Quran are created indicators of that uncreated attribute.

?
Nasafi uses the analogy of Allah's names: the name 'Allah' has parts (hamza, lam, etc.) and is created, yet points to the one essence. Are you ever guilty of revering the Arabic sounds or the Mushaf more than the uncreated attribute they point to? What concrete step will you take to ensure your reverence is directed to the eternal speech itself?
?
If the eternal attribute is one and indivisible, yet you only encounter its commands and prohibitions through created expressions in time, how does that urgency shape your daily obedience? What will change in your life today to act on the command behind the words you recite?

The Maturidi Position on Created Expressions
Nasafi summarizes the Maturidi view on created expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi summarizes the Maturidi view that Quranic expressions are created indications of God's eternal speech.

Core Idea
Created expressions point to God's eternal speech; they are not the speech itself.

Nasafi begins by summarizing the Maturidi response to the Mu'tazilite claim that the Quran is created. He distinguishes between the created expressions (alphaz) and the eternal attribute of speech they signify. This lesson unpacks that summary and clarifies the real point of contention.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi summary
Read the Arabic passage up to 'هذا هو تلخيص المذهب' and its translation. Notice how Nasafi describes the created expressions as indications of two things: linguistic/historical meanings and God's own mention and command.
The term 'دلالات' (indications) is key.
2
Identify the two layers of indication
First, the expressions indicate created meanings (stories, events). Second, they indicate God's eternal speech (His mention and command). Understand that the expressions are not the speech itself but signs pointing to it.
3
Understand the disputed point
Nasafi states that the real dispute is about 'the meaning of His speech' (المعنى بكلامه) – the attribute of speech subsisting in God's essence. This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجملة الجواب أن هذه العبارات مخلوقة، وهي دلالات على المعاني اللغوية والأشخاص وأحوالها كشخص فرعون وغرقة وما تفوه به، ويوسف(۲) وإخوته عليهم السلام وإلقائهم إياه في الجب وغير ذلك. وهذا كله مخلوق وهي أيضا دلالات على ذكر الله تعالى إياها وإخباره عنها ، وأمره بما أمر، ونهيه عما نهى وذلك هو المعنى بكلامه وهو المتنازع فيه. هذا هو تلخيص المذهب. ثم المعتزلة لا يثبتون هذه الصفة القائمة بذات الله تعالى لاستحالة قيام صفة بذات الله تعالى فكانت المسألة في الحقيقة عين (۳) مسألة الصفات ثم اضطروا إذا لم يمكنهم إثبات صفة قائمة بالذات تصير الألفاظ عبارات عنها تسوية بين الغائب والشاهد إلى جعل عين هذه الألفاظ والعبارات كلاما ( ٤ ) (من غير ) (٥) أن تكون هي دلالات على المعنى القائم بالذات فخالفوا الشاهد ثم ادعوا علينا مخالفته وارتكبوا المحال، فإن عندهم
And the sum of the answer is that these expressions are created, and they are indications of linguistic meanings, persons, and their states, such as the person of Pharaoh, his drowning, and what he uttered, and Joseph and his brothers, peace be upon them, and their throwing him into the well, and other than that. All this is created. And they are also indications of Allah the Exalted's mention of them, His informing about them, His commanding what He commanded, and His prohibiting what He prohibited, and that is the meaning of His speech, and it is that which is disputed. This is the summary of the school. Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm this attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted due to the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted, so the issue in reality is the same as the issue of attributes. Then when they could not affirm an attribute subsisting in the essence such that the expressions become expressions for it, making equality between the unseen and the witnessed, they were forced to make these very expressions and phrases speech, without them being indications of the meaning subsisting in the essence. So they contradicted the witnessed and then claimed upon us that we contradict it, and they committed the impossible. For according to them
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage summarizes the Maturidi position on the createdness of the Quranic expressions and refutes the Mu'tazilite view. Nasafi states that all the expressions in the Quran, including stories of past prophets and events, are created. They serve as indications of God's speech in two ways: they indicate the linguistic meanings and historical events, and they indicate God's mention and command. The real subject of dispute is the attribute of speech itself. The Mu'tazila deny any attribute subsisting in God's essence, including speech. Because they cannot affirm such an attribute, they are forced to claim that the created expressions themselves are God's speech, without being indications of a meaning in God's essence. In doing so, they contradict both reason and analogy (qiyas) between the unseen and witnessed. By making the expressions themselves speech, they commit logical impossibility.

Key Terms
دلالات
كلام
مخلوق
المتنازع فيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what do the created expressions indicate?
A) Only historical events
B) Only linguistic meanings
C) Both linguistic/historical meanings and God's mention/command
D) Nothing; they are mere sounds

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position distinguishes between created expressions and the eternal attribute of speech they indicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you consciously distinguish between the created words and the eternal speech they point to? How would your reverence change if you truly felt that every command and prohibition you recite originates from God's uncreated attribute?
?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Denial of the Attribute
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite denial of the attribute of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazilite denial of the attribute of speech, and why they are forced into an impossible position.

Core Idea
Denying attributes leads to contradiction and logical impossibility.

After summarizing the Maturidi view, Nasafi turns to the Mu'tazilite position. He explains that their denial of a subsisting attribute forces them to identify God's speech with created expressions, which violates the principle of analogy between the unseen and witnessed. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite denial
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم المعتزلة' to the end of the chunk. Note how Nasafi describes their denial of an attribute subsisting in God's essence.
2
Understand the forced conclusion
Because they cannot affirm a subsisting attribute, they are forced to make the created expressions themselves into God's speech, without them being indications of a meaning in the essence.
This is 'جعل عين هذه الألفاظ والعبارات كلاما'.
3
Recognize the contradiction
Nasafi points out that in doing so, they contradict the witnessed world (where expressions indicate meanings) and commit impossibility (محال). Understand the charge of violating qiyas al-ghaib 'ala al-shahid.
The argument is that they deny the analogy between unseen and witnessed.

This is the summary of the school. Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm this attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted due to the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted, so the issue in reality is the same as the issue of attributes. Then when they could not affirm an attribute subsisting in the essence such that the expressions become expressions for it, making equality between the unseen and the witnessed, they were forced to make these very expressions and phrases speech, without them being indications of the meaning subsisting in the essence. So they contradicted the witnessed and then claimed upon us that we contradict it, and they committed the impossible. For according to them
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage summarizes the Maturidi position on the createdness of the Quranic expressions and refutes the Mu'tazilite view. Nasafi states that all the expressions in the Quran, including stories of past prophets and events, are created. They serve as indications of God's speech in two ways: they indicate the linguistic meanings and historical events, and they indicate God's mention and command. The real subject of dispute is the attribute of speech itself. The Mu'tazila deny any attribute subsisting in God's essence, including speech. Because they cannot affirm such an attribute, they are forced to claim that the created expressions themselves are God's speech, without being indications of a meaning in God's essence. In doing so, they contradict both reason and analogy (qiyas) between the unseen and witnessed. By making the expressions themselves speech, they commit logical impossibility.

غائب
شاهد

Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that the Quran is created and that God's speech is identical to these created expressions.
Correction: Nasafi argues that this contradicts the witnessed world where expressions indicate meanings, thus the Mu'tazila fail to maintain consistency between unseen and witnessed.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why were the Mu'tazila forced to make the expressions themselves into God's speech?
A) Because they believed speech is eternal
B) Because they could not affirm a subsisting attribute
C) Because they accepted the Maturidi position
D) Because they wanted to honor the Quran

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite denial of the attribute of speech leads them to an impossible position that contradicts reason and analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila denied an attribute because they could not reconcile it with their rational framework. Are there aspects of Allah's attributes that you struggle to accept because they challenge your own assumptions? What would it cost you to submit to them fully, even when they seem 'impossible' to your mind?

The Hanbali View and Its Analogy
Al-Nasafi presents the Hanbali view with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanbali claim that Quranic expressions are not created and how they use the analogy of God's mention and writing to clarify their position.

Core Idea
Quranic expressions are not created but indicate the essence without containing it.

Before presenting the Maturidi position, Al-Nasafi sets forth the Hanbali view on the createdness of Quranic expressions. The Hanbalis argue that while the Quran is written in codices and recited by tongues, these physical acts are mere indicators of the divine speech, not the speech itself. They illustrate this with the analogy of Allah being mentioned or written: the mention or writing is not His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hanbali position
Read the first part of the Arabic passage that describes the Hanbali view. Notice how they affirm the Quran is written, recited, and preserved, yet not present in those mediums.
The key is the phrase 'غير حال فيها' meaning not indwelling.
2
Understand the analogy
The Hanbalis draw an analogy with how Allah is mentioned by tongues, worshipped in prayer niches, and written on paper. These are acts that point to Allah, but Allah does not dwell in them. Similarly, Quranic expressions point to God's speech without containing it.
The analogy shows that the expressions are signs, not the essence.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: Why do the Hanbalis emphasize this distinction? They want to affirm that the Quran is uncreated while avoiding the conclusion that God's essence is physically present in creation.
This sets the stage for the Maturidi response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهم السامع أن هذه العبارات المتركبة من الحروف والأصوات ليست بمخلوقة، كما يقوله الحنابلة، ويقولون : إن القرآن مكتوب في مصاحفنا، مقروء بألسنتنا، محفوظ في صدورنا، غير حال فيها، أي: الكتابة الدالة عليه في مصاحفنا، والقراءة الدالة عليه في ألسنتنا ، وحفظ الألفاظ الدالة عليه في صدورنا لا ذاته. كما يقال : الله تعالى مذكور بألسنتنا معبود في محاريبنا، وهو مكتوب على هذه الكاغدة. ولم يرد بذلك حلول ذاته في الألسنة والمحاريب والكاغدة، بل وجود ذكره وعبادته وكتابة العبارات الدالة على ذاته دون ذاته. فكذا هذا. رأي النسفي مختصرا: وجملة الجواب أن هذه العبارات مخلوقة، وهي دلالات على المعاني اللغوية والأشخاص وأحوالها كشخص فرعون وغرقة وما تفوه به، ويوسف وإخوته عليهم السلام وإلقائهم إياه في الجب وغير ذلك. وهذا كله مخلوق وهي أيضا دلالات على ذكر الله تعالى إياها وإخباره عنها ، وأمره بما أمر، ونهيه عما نهى وذلك هو المعنى بكلامه وهو المتنازع فيه. هذا هو تلخيص المذهب.
And the understanding of the listener is that these expressions composed of letters and sounds are not created, as the Hanabila say, and they say: Indeed the Quran is written in our codices, recited by our tongues, preserved in our hearts, not however present in them, meaning: the writing indicating it in our codices, and the recitation indicating it on our tongues, and the preservation of the utterances indicating it in our hearts, not its essence. Just as it is said: Allah the Exalted is mentioned by our tongues, worshipped in our prayer niches, and He is written on this paper. But by that is not intended the indwelling of His essence in the tongues, prayer niches, and paper, rather the existence of His mention, worship, and the writing of expressions indicating Him without His essence. So too is this. The view of al-Nasafi summarized: The gist of the answer is that these expressions are created, and they are indicators of the linguistic meanings and persons and their states, such as the person of Pharaoh and his drowning and what he uttered, and Yusuf and his brothers, peace be upon them, and their throwing him into the well, and other than that. All of this is created, and they are also indicators of Allah the Exalted's mentioning of them, His informing about them, His commanding what He commanded, and His forbidding what He forbade, and that is the meaning of His speech, and it is that which is disputed. This is the summary of the school.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting the Hanbali view that the Quranic expressions (letters and sounds) are not created, but clarifies that the writing, recitation, and memorization are not the essence—they merely indicate it, similar to how God is mentioned or written but not present. Then al-Nasafi gives the Maturidi summary: the expressions are created and serve as indicators (دلالات) for linguistic meanings, historical events (e.g., Pharaoh, Yusuf), and also indicate God's own mention and commands. That intended meaning is the real divine speech (كلام الله) and is the point of contention. Thus, the Maturidi school distinguishes between the created expressions and the uncreated meaning subsisting in God's essence.

Key Terms
عبارات
دلالات
غير حال فيها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanbali view presented, what is the status of the writing of the Quran in codices?
A) It is the very essence of the Quran.
B) It is an indicator of the Quran, not its essence.
C) It is created.
D) It is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Hanbalis use an analogy to show that Quranic expressions are unmade indicators of God's speech, not the speech itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you touch the mushaf or recite the Quran, do you ever find yourself acting as if the paper or your voice contains God—even in subtle ways? How would your reverence change if you consistently remembered that these are mere indicators, yet the Quran is truly uncreated and from Him?
?
The analogy equates saying 'Allah is written on paper' with saying 'the Quran is written in codices'. Do you treat the physical Quran with the same awe as if Allah’s essence were inside it, or do you sometimes become casual, forgetting it is an indicator of His speech? What commitment can you make to honor that distinction?
?
If you fail to hold this distinction—that the Quran's expressions are not created and do not house God's essence—does your heart subtly confuse creation with Creator? Search your soul: where have you taken the Words of Allah lightly, and what will you change today?

The Maturidi Summary: Expressions Created, Meaning Uncreated
Al-Nasafi summarizes the Maturidi answer distinguishing created expressions from uncreated meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position as summarized by Al-Nasafi: Quranic expressions (letters and sounds) are created, while the divine speech they indicate is the uncreated meaning subsisting in God's essence.

Core Idea
Quranic expressions are created indicators of God's uncreated speech.

After presenting the Hanbali view, Al-Nasafi gives the Maturidi summary. He states that the expressions themselves are created and serve as indicators (دلالات) for two things: the linguistic meanings and historical accounts (like Pharaoh and Yusuf), and also for God's own mentioning and commanding. The real divine speech is the meaning, which is the point of contention. This lesson unpacks that concise summary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maturidi claim
Read the second part of the passage where Al-Nasafi begins 'وجملة الجواب'. Note the clear statement: 'these expressions are created'.
This is the core Maturidi position.
2
Understand the two levels of indication
The expressions first indicate linguistic meanings and historical persons (like Pharaoh, Yusuf). Second, they indicate God's own speech (His mentioning, informing, commanding). All these expressions are created, but the intended meaning (God's speech) is uncreated.
The expressions point to both created stories and uncreated divine speech.
3
Grasp the point of dispute
Al-Nasafi concludes that the meaning of God's speech is 'that which is disputed'. The Maturidis affirm that God's speech (the meaning) is an eternal attribute, while the verbal expressions are created and thus not part of the eternal attribute.
This resolves the createdness debate.

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Denial of God's Speech
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing their position leads to nullifying the Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical consequences of the Mu'tazilite denial of the attribute of speech and how Al-Nasafi refutes them using the analogy of the witnessed world.

Core Idea
Denying God's attribute of speech makes all divine commands meaningless.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi confronts the Mu'tazilite claim that God's speech is merely letters and sounds without an eternal attribute. He points out a fundamental inconsistency: their own principles require that speech without internal meaning is not valid, yet they apply the opposite to God. By the end, you will see why this position leads to the nullification of the entire Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the argument builds from the Mu'tazilite denial to the final conclusion.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Identify what the Mu'tazila affirm and deny about God's speech. They deny an attribute subsisting in God's essence and claim the utterances themselves are speech.
3
Grasp the analogy from the witnessed world
Follow the example of a person uttering words without internal meaning. Why does the author use this to refute the Mu'tazila?
The analogy shows that in the witnessed world, such utterances are not considered genuine speech.
4
See the consequence
Reflect on the conclusion: if God's speech is just sounds, then no command or prohibition has force, nullifying all divine laws. Consider the author's strong condemnation.
The author states that such a view removes one from religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعتزلة لا يثبتون هذه الصفة القائمة بذات الله تعالى لاستحالة قيام صفة بذات الله تعالى فكانت المسألة في الحقيقة عين مسألة الصفات ثم اضطروا إذا لم يمكنهم إثبات صفة قائمة بالذات تصير الألفاظ عبارات عنها تسوية بين الغائب والشاهد إلى جعل عين هذه الألفاظ والعبارات كلاما من غير أن تكون هي دلالات على المعنى القائم بالذات فخالفوا الشاهد ثم ادعوا علينا مخالفته وارتكبوا المحال، فإن عندهم العبارة فقط لا تكون معتبرة إذا لم تكن دالة على معنى قائم بالنفس، وهم جعلوها معتبرة وهو خلاف المعقول. ألا ترى أن في الشاهد لو أجرى ذات صفة الأمر والنهي والخبر على اللسان وثبت لنا بالدليل الضروري أن ليس في نفس المجري معنى الأمر والنهي والخبر، وليست هذه الألفاظ بأداء لتلك المعاني ولا بعبارة عنها. أن ذلك لم يعد كلاما ولا أمرا ولا نهيا ولا خبرا، ولا يعتقد وجوب ما يتناوله لفظة الأمر ولا حرمة ما تتناوله لفظة النهي ولا ثبوت ما تتناوله لفظة الخبر، فلو كان الأمر على ما زعمت المعتزلة، في استحالة قيام الكلام بالذات، ووجوب الحروف والأصوات في محل من غير أن يكون شيء منها دليلا على صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى وإيجاب حظرهما صفتيه، لم تكن هذه الحروف البتة كلاما، ولا وجب بها شيء ولا حظر بها فعل وتعطلت الشرائع بأسرها فالقائل به منسلخ عن الدين، بل عن الأديان السماوية ويلتحق بالدهرية والمعطلة والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عن الوقوع في مثله، بمنه وفضله.
Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm this attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted due to the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted, so the issue is in reality the very issue of attributes. Then they were forced, when they could not affirm an attribute subsisting in the essence, so that the utterances become expressions of it, making a comparison between the unseen and the witnessed, to make these very utterances and expressions speech, without them being indicators of the meaning subsisting in the essence. Thus they contradicted the witnessed, then they claimed that we contradicted it, and they committed the impossible. For according to them, an expression alone is not considered valid if it does not indicate a meaning residing in the soul, yet they made it considered valid, which is contrary to reason. Do you not see that in the witnessed, if a person delivers on the tongue something having the form of command, prohibition, and report, and it is established for us by necessary proof that there is no meaning of command, prohibition, and report in the soul of the deliverer, and these utterances are not conveying those meanings nor are they expressions of them, then that is not considered speech, nor command, nor prohibition, nor report. Nor is the obligation of what the word of command indicates, nor the prohibition of what the word of prohibition indicates, nor the affirmation of what the word of report indicates, believed. If the matter were as the Mu'tazila claim, regarding the impossibility of speech subsisting in the essence, and the necessity of letters and sounds in a locus without any of them being evidence of an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah the Exalted, and the imposition of its prohibition as two attributes, then these letters would not be speech at all, nor would any obligation arise from them, nor would any act be forbidden by them, and all the divine laws would be nullified. Thus the one who says this is stripped of religion, indeed of the heavenly religions, and joins the materialists and the negators. And praise be to Allah who protected us from falling into the like of it, by His grace and favor.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite position by first stating that they deny the attribute of speech subsisting in God's essence, leading them to claim that the letters and sounds themselves are speech without indicating any meaning in God. The author points out a contradiction: according to the Mu'tazila themselves, a mere expression without a corresponding meaning in the soul is not considered valid speech in the witnessed world (al-shahid). Yet they apply the opposite to God. The argument uses an analogy: if someone utters words that have the form of command, prohibition, or report but without any internal intention, such utterances are not genuine commands or prohibitions. Similarly, if God's speech were merely letters and sounds without an attribute in His essence, then no command or prohibition would be binding, rendering the entire Sharia void. The author concludes that such a view removes one from religion and aligns with materialists (dahriyya) and negators (mu'attila).

القائم بالذات
الشاهد
الدهرية
المعطلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite view is just a different opinion about the nature of God's speech.
Correction: The author demonstrates that their view logically entails that no command or prohibition from God has any binding force, rendering all divine laws void.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that letters and sounds are speech without a meaning in God's essence?
A) The testimony of the Quran.
B) The analogy of the witnessed world where utterances without internal meaning are not considered valid speech.
C) The consensus of the Companions.
D) The logical impossibility of a self-subsisting essence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite denial of the attribute of speech ultimately destroys the basis of all divine commands and prohibitions, removing one from the fold of religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the Quran, do you truly feel that you are encountering the eternal speech of Allah, or do you treat it as mere words? What would change in your heart if you were fully convinced that every command and prohibition originates from an attribute of Allah Himself?
?
If you were to act as if Allah's commands were empty—like the Mu'tazilite view implies—how would your daily obedience change? In which areas of your life have you been treating Allah's speech as less than absolute, perhaps by delaying or ignoring His commands?
?
Al-Nasafi warns that denying God's speech strips one of religion. Examine your own convictions: do you hold any subtle doubts about the authority of the Quran as God's real speech? What would it cost you to surrender completely to every verse as binding on your soul?

Maturidi View on the Descent of the Quran
Author responds to an objection about the Quran not descending upon Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on the descent of the Quran and how they distinguish their view from that of the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The Quran's descent does not mean God's attribute separates from Him.

This passage addresses a common Mu'tazilite accusation that the Maturidis deny the Quran's descent. The author refutes this by showing that even leading Mu'tazilites affirm that God's speech is in the Preserved Tablet, and the difference is in what the revealed expressions convey. This lesson clarifies the nuanced Maturidi perspective on the relationship between the eternal attribute of speech and the revealed text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila attack the Maturidis claiming they deny the Quran descended upon Muhammad because attributes cannot separate from their subject.
This objection is based on a misunderstanding of the Maturidi position.
2
Identify the refutation
The author responds by naming prominent Mu'tazilites (Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, al-Ka'bi) who themselves affirm that God's speech is in the Preserved Tablet and the revealed Quran is expressions.
This shows that the objection is inconsistent with some Mu'tazilite views.
3
Compare the two positions
Mu'tazila say the expressions convey what is in the Preserved Tablet. Maturidis say they convey the attribute of speech subsisting in God's essence.
The key difference lies in what the expressions point to.
4
Grasp the central difference
For Maturidis, the eternal attribute of speech remains with Allah, and the revealed expressions are not the attribute itself but indicate it.
This preserves divine transcendence while affirming revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما شنع علينا بعض أغبيائهم ، أنكم تقولون : لم ينزل القرآن على محمد عليه السلام لأن الصفة لا تزايل الموصوف مردود عليهم، لأن جعفر بن حرب وجعفر بن مبشر والكعبي يزعمون أن كلام الله في اللوح المحفوظ والمنزل عبارات دالة عليه على حسب ما نقوله نحن، غير أنهم يقولون: إن هذه العبارات مؤدية ما في اللوح المحفوظ، ونحن نقول : هي مؤدية ما قام بالذات من الصفة.
And some of their ignorant ones attacked us, saying: You claim that the Quran was not sent down upon Muhammad (peace be upon him) because the attribute does not separate from the described. This is refuted against them, because Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and al-Ka'bi claim that the speech of Allah is in the Preserved Tablet, and what is sent down is expressions signifying it, according to what we also say. However, they say that these expressions convey what is in the Preserved Tablet, while we say they convey what subsists in the essence of the attribute.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The Mu'tazilites attack the Maturidis with the claim that they deny the Quran descended upon Muhammad, based on the principle that an attribute cannot separate from its subject. The author responds by pointing out that prominent Mu'tazilites such as Ja'far ibn Harb, Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and al-Ka'bi themselves affirm that God's speech is located in the Preserved Tablet, and that the revealed Quran consists of expressions indicating that speech. The difference lies in what these expressions convey: the Mu'tazilites hold that they convey the speech in the Preserved Tablet, while the Maturidis hold that they convey the attribute of speech subsisting in God's essence.

Key Terms
النزول (descent)
اللوح المحفوظ (Preserved Tablet)
الصفة (attribute)
العبارات (expressions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidis deny the Quran's descent entirely.
Correction: The Maturidis affirm the descent of expressions that indicate the eternal attribute of speech; they do not deny the descent but offer a nuanced explanation.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidis, what do the expressions of the Quran convey?
A) The speech in the Preserved Tablet.
B) The attribute of speech subsisting in God's essence.
C) God's will.
D) Temporal created things.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis affirm the Quran's descent as expressions pointing to the eternal attribute of speech, not as the attribute itself moving.

The Impossibility of One Act Having Two Agents
Refutation of the claim that the recited Quran is God's act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction in the Mu'tazilite claim that the recited Quran is both God's speech and act and the human's act.

Core Idea
One act cannot have two agents.

This lesson examines a key refutation by Imam al-Nasafi against the Mu'tazilite position on the Quran. The Mu'tazila claimed that the very words recited by a human are God's speech and act. But this leads to a contradiction: if it is God's act, it cannot also be the human's act, because one act cannot have two agents. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author states the opponent's view first, then refutes it.
The passage is concise; every phrase matters.
2
Identify the two impossibilities
The author points out two impossible consequences: (1) one act having two agents, and (2) one speech being attributed to two speakers. Find these in the text.
These are the core of the refutation.
3
Understand how the Mu'tazila contradict themselves
The Mu'tazila themselves deny that one act can have two agents, yet their claim about the recited Quran violates this principle. See how the author highlights this inconsistency.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يقوله الإسكافي والجبائي وعامة المعتزلة : أن عين ما يقرأ واحد منا هو كلام الله تعالى وفعله فهو فاسد، لأن القارئ يقرأ باختباره وهو فعله حتى إنه يثاب عليه، فلو كان هذا فعل الله تعالى لكان فعلا لفاعلين، وكلاما لمتكلمين، فصاروا بدعوتهم أنه فعل فاعلين مناقضين، لأنهم يزعمون أنه محال أن يكون فعل ما مفعول لفاعلين، ومقدور ما مقدور القادرين ، ولهذا أنكروا أن تكون أفعال العباد مخلوقة الله تعالى وبجعلهم إياه كلاما لمتكلمين صاروا خارجين عن إجماع العقلاء، لأن أحدا لم يستجز أن يكون كلام واحد وصفا لمتكلمين يصيران به متكلمين، فأبطلوا بهذا ما جعلوه أصلا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، فإنهم يعدون كون شيء فعلا لفاعلين محالا قياسا على استحالة كون كلام كلاما لمتكلمين.
As for what al-Iskafi, al-Jubba'i, and the generality of the Mu'tazila say, that the very same thing that one of us recites is the speech of Allah and His act, it is false, because the reader recites by his choice and it is his act, such that he is rewarded for it. If this were the act of Allah, it would be an act of two agents and speech of two speakers. Thus by claiming it is an act of two agents they contradict themselves, because they claim it is impossible for one thing to be the effect of two agents, and for one thing to be the power of two powerful ones, and for this reason they deny that the acts of servants are created by Allah. And by making it speech of two speakers, they go outside the consensus of the rational people, because no one permits that one speech be an attribute of two speakers by which they become speakers. Thus they nullify what they took as a principle in the question of the creation of acts, for they consider it impossible for a thing to be an act of two agents, by analogy with the impossibility of one speech being speech of two speakers.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author addresses the claim of al-Iskafi, al-Jubba'i, and the general Mu'tazila that the very thing recited by a human is the speech and act of Allah. The proof against this is that the reader recites by his own choice and is rewarded, so the recitation is his act. If it were also God's act, then one act would have two agents, which is impossible by the Mu'tazilites' own principle. They themselves deny that acts of servants are created by Allah based on this impossibility. Furthermore, one speech cannot be attributed to two speakers. By making the recited Quran speech of both God and the human, they contradict their own rule against one act having two agents, thus nullifying their doctrine on the creation of acts.

Key Terms
الفعل
الفاعل
الكلام
المتكلم
الخلق
الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether the Quran is created or uncreated.
Correction: The argument is specifically about agency: whether the recited Quran can be the act of both God and the human simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What logical contradiction does the author identify in the Mu'tazilite position?
A) They affirm free will but deny human acts.
B) They claim one speech cannot be for two speakers, yet make the recited Quran speech of both God and the reader.
C) They deny the Quran is created but affirm it is speech.
D) They believe in reward but deny human effort.

Key Takeaway
The recited Quran cannot be both God's act and the human's act, because that would require two agents for one act, which is logically impossible by the Mu'tazila's own admission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly feel it is both your effort and Allah's speech? How does realizing that one act cannot have two agents reshape your sense of humility before the Quran?

Are there areas of your life where you attribute your good deeds solely to yourself, forgetting that Allah is the ultimate Creator of all actions? Will you actively remind yourself of this truth after this lesson?
?
This passage exposes the danger of holding contradictory beliefs. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah or your own actions that might contradict each other? Are you willing to examine them honestly and correct them?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Identity of Recitation
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite identity claim with logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that the reader's recitation is identical to God's speech, and how this leads to a contradiction with their own principles.

Core Idea
A single act cannot have two agents.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi responds to a key Mu'tazilite doctrine: that the human recitation of the Qur'an is exactly God's speech and act. He shows that this claim forces the Mu'tazilites to violate their own foundational principle that an act cannot have two agents. Let's examine how Nasafi's refutation unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Read Nasafi's opening statement summarising the Mu'tazilite position and his verdict that it is corrupt.
Note the key phrase: 'anin ma yaqra' (the very thing recited).
2
Understand the argument from reward
Nasafi points out that the reader recites by choice and is rewarded, showing it is the reader's act. If it were also God's act, it would be an act of two agents.
This directly contradicts the Mu'tazilite principle that an act cannot have two agents.
3
See the contradiction and consensus
Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazilites thereby undermine their own stance on the creation of acts, and they depart from rational consensus that one speech cannot belong to two speakers.
This step completes the refutation by showing the inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يقوله الإسكافي والجبائي وعامة المعتزلة: أن عين ما يقرأ واحد منا هو كلام الله تعالى وفعله فهو فاسد، لأن القارئ يقرأ باختباره وهو فعله حتى إنه يثاب عليه، فلو كان هذا فعل الله تعالى لكان فعلا لفاعلين، وكلاما لمتكلمين، فصاروا بدعوتهم أنه فعل فاعلين مناقضين، لأنهم يزعمون أنه محال أن يكون فعل ما مفعول لفاعلين، ومقدور ما مقدور القادرين، ولهذا أنكروا أن تكون أفعال العباد مخلوقة الله تعالى وبجعلهم إياه كلاما لمتكلمين صاروا خارجين عن إجماع العقلاء، لأن أحدا لم يستجز أن يكون كلام واحد وصفا لمتكلمين يصيران به متكلمين، فأبطلوا بهذا ما جعلوه أصلا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، فإنهم يعدون كون شيء فعلا لفاعلين محالا قياسا على استحالة كون كلام كلاما لمتكلمين
As for what al-Iskafi and al-Jubba'i and the generality of the Mu'tazila say: that the very thing that one of us recites is the speech of God Most High and His act, it is corrupt. Because the reader recites by his choice and it is his act such that he is rewarded for it. If this were the act of God Most High, it would be an act of two agents and speech of two speakers. Thus they become, by their claim that it is an act of two agents, inconsistent, because they claim that it is impossible for a thing to be an act of two agents and a thing of power of two powerful ones. For this reason they denied that the acts of servants are created by God Most High. And by making it speech of two speakers, they depart from the consensus of the rational people, because no one has permitted that one speech be an attribute of two speakers by which they become speakers. Thus they nullified what they made as a principle in the issue of the creation of acts, for they consider a thing being an act of two agents impossible, by analogy to the impossibility of one speech being speech of two speakers.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that the very recitation of the Qur'an by a human is identical to God's speech and act. The author, Imam Nasafi, argues that this leads to a contradiction. He points out that the reader recites by his own choice and is rewarded for it, so it is his act. If it were also God's act, then it would be an act of two agents and speech of two speakers. The Mu'tazilites themselves claim that an act cannot have two agents, which is why they deny that God creates human acts. By asserting that this recitation is both God's act and the human's act, they contradict their own principle. Furthermore, no rational person allows that one speech is the attribute of two speakers. Thus the Mu'tazilites have undermined their own principle in the issue of the creation of acts, because they consider an act of two agents impossible, just as they consider one speech being speech of two speakers impossible.

كلام الله
فعل فاعلين
كلام لمتكلمين

Nasafi’s Critique of al-Basri on God’s Speech
Nasafi critiques al-Basri's view, showing it implies God's speech is a perishing accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that al-Basri's view reduces God's speech to a temporal accident that perishes.

Core Idea
Al-Basri’s view makes God’s speech a passing accident, not eternal.

In this passage, Nasafi directly critiques Abu al-Husayn al-Basri’s linguistic approach to the divine speech. He exposes how al-Basri’s analogy with human speech implies that God’s original utterance was a transient accident that subsequently ceased to exist. By comparing God’s speech to poetry and sermons, Nasafi shows the logical consequence of al-Basri’s position: the reciter produces a similar but not identical speech. Let’s follow his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting how Nasafi structures his critique.
The passage begins with 'Then whoever ponders...'
2
Identify al-Basri's analogy
Notice that al-Basri compares God's speech to the Prophet's speech. Ask: why does Nasafi reject this?
3
Understand the implication
Nasafi states that al-Basri views both as accidents that cannot endure. Grasp why this makes God's speech perishing.
4
See the analogy with poems
Nasafi extends the analogy: like a poem's original composition perishes, subsequent recitations are similar. Recognize the point.
The passage ends abruptly, but the main critique is clear.
5
Reflect on the critique
Summarize in your own words how Nasafi undermines al-Basri's position.
Connect to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من تأمل ما ألزمته عرف أن هذا ليس بانفصال عنه لما مر أن هذا لا يخلو من الأوجه الثلاثة على نحو ما قسَّمنا وأبطلنا كل قسم. ثم اعتباره هذا بما اعتبر من كلام النبي عليه السلام دليل أنه يعتقد أن هذا ليس بكلام الله تعالى، بل كلام الله تعالى وجد ثم تلاشى ككلام النبي عليه السلام لاستوائهما عنده في كونهما عرضين يستحيل عليهما البقاء، وبين المراد بقولهم: هو كلام الله تعالى أن الله تعالى هو الذي سبق إليه فتكلم بهذا اللفظ على هذا النظم ثم انعدم ذلك، فيتكلم المتكلم بمثل ذلك لا يعين ذلك كما في كلام النبي عليه السلام، وهذا هو الحكم في كل قصيدة وخطبة ورسالة أن المنشئ هو الذي تولى ذلك النظم ثم ذهب وتلاشى وفنيت صفاته، ومن ينشد تلك القصيدة أو يقرأ تلك الخطبة والرسالة ينشد ويقرأ على ذلك النظم، فيكون متكلما بمثل ما تكلم به الناظم الأول، لا أن
Then whoever ponders what I have required of him knows that this is not a separation from it, because it has passed that this does not go beyond the three aspects in the manner we divided and we nullified each division. Then his analogy of this with what was considered from the speech of the Prophet, peace be upon him, is evidence that he believes that this is not the speech of God Most High; rather, the speech of God Most High existed then passed away like the speech of the Prophet, peace be upon him, because in his view they are equal in being accidents that cannot endure. And he explained the meaning of their saying 'it is the speech of God Most High' as that God Most High is the one who preceded to it and spoke this wording in this order, then that ceased to exist. So the speaker speaks the like of it, not the very same, as in the speech of the Prophet, peace be upon him. And this is the ruling for every poem, sermon, and letter: that the originator is the one who undertook that order, then it went and passed away and its attributes perished. And whoever recites that poem or reads that sermon and letter recites and reads according to that order, so he becomes a speaker of the like of what the first composer spoke. Not that...
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a critique of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's linguistic solution. Nasafi points out that al-Basri's view does not escape the previous objections, because the issue necessarily falls into one of the three aspects that Nasafi had already refuted. Furthermore, al-Basri's comparison to the speech of the Prophet shows that he believes God's speech is not actually present in the recitation; instead, God's speech originally existed and then perished, similar to human speech. For al-Basri, both God's speech and the Prophet's speech are accidents that cannot endure. He interprets the phrase 'this is the speech of God' to mean that God was the first to utter this wording and order, and that original utterance ceased to exist. The reciter then speaks something similar, not the exact same speech. Nasafi explains that this is how all poems, sermons, and letters are treated: the original author's composition passes away, and later reciters produce similar speech. The passage ends incompletely with 'not that...' (la an), likely continuing to clarify that the reciter does not produce the exact same speech.

Key Terms
الأوجه الثلاثة
عرضين
الانعدام
الناظم الأول
النظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Basri's view preserves God's speech as eternal.
Correction: Nasafi shows that al-Basri's view reduces it to a temporal accident that perishes, making it non-eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's critique, what does al-Basri's view imply about God's speech?
A) It is eternal and uncreated.
B) It is an accident that perishes and is then imitated.
C) It is identical to the reader's recitation.
D) It is a continuous attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that al-Basri's analogy fails because it treats God's speech as a transient accident, contradicting the eternal nature of God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Analogy of Divine Speech with Prophetic Speech
Nasafi explains the attribution of speech to its original speaker using the analogy of prophetic speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that calling the Qur'an 'the speech of God' means God is the original speaker, not that the recited words are identical to an eternal attribute.

Core Idea
When we say 'this is God's speech', we mean God originated it; the reciter speaks something like it.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a critical question: what does it mean to call the recited Qur'an 'the speech of God'? He argues that it does not imply that the recited words are the very same as an eternal attribute; rather, God is the first to speak them, and the reciter utters something similar. This distinction refutes opponents who deny the eternity of God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice how Nasafi builds his argument from linguistic practice to a theological conclusion.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the linguistic practice
Nasafi begins by noting that people attribute speech to the original speaker, e.g., 'this is the speech of the Prophet' even when recited by others. Similarly, Muslims say 'this is the speech of God' when reciting the Qur'an.
3
Understand the opponent's implication
Nasafi argues that the opponent (who denies the eternity of God's speech) must hold that God's speech existed and then perished, like human speech. This leads to the conclusion that the recited Qur'an is not actually God's speech.
The opponent's view makes God's speech a perishable accident.
4
Grasp Nasafi's analogy and conclusion
Nasafi explains the correct meaning: God originated the speech in a particular arrangement, then it ceased. The reciter speaks something like it, not the very same, just as a poem is attributed to its originator even when recited by others. Therefore, the recitation is 'like' God's speech, not the very speech.
This preserves the divinity of the Qur'an while avoiding the problem of accidents enduring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل اللغة: إن هذا الكلام كلام النبي عليه السلام، فيضيفونه إلى أول من تكلم به، وكذلك إذا تلا الواحد منا القرآن أن يقول: هذا كلام الله تعالى بمعنى أن الله تعالى أول من تكلم به والناس يتلونه ويحتذون عليه. ثم من تأمل ما ألزمته عرف أن هذا ليس بانفصال عنه لما مر أن هذا لا يخلو من الأوجه الثلاثة على نحو ما قسَّمنا وأبطلنا كل قسم. ثم اعتباره هذا بما اعتبر من كلام النبي عليه السلام دليل أنه يعتقد أن هذا ليس بكلام الله تعالى، بل كلام الله تعالى وجد ثم تلاشى ككلام النبي عليه السلام لاستوائهما عنده في كونهما عرضين يستحيل عليهما البقاء، وبين المراد بقولهم: هو كلام الله تعالى أن الله تعالى هو الذي سبق إليه فتكلم بهذا اللفظ على هذا النظم ثم انعدم ذلك، فيتكلم المتكلم بمثل ذلك لا بعين ذلك كما في كلام النبي عليه السلام، وهذا هو الحكم في كل قصيدة وخطبة ورسالة أن المنشئ هو الذي تولى ذلك النظم ثم ذهب وتلاشى وفنيت صفاته، ومن ينشد تلك القصيدة أو يقرأ تلك الخطبة والرسالة ينشد ويقرأ على ذلك النظم، فيكون متكلما بمثل ما تكلم به الناظم الأول، لا أن يكون ذلك حقيقة كلام الناظم الأول بإجماع العقلاء باستحالة بقاء كلام الله تعالى، وكل كلام، وبما قام من دليل استحالة القول ببقاء الأعراض. وجاء من هذا أن قراءة القارئ ينبغي أن تكون عندهم مثل كلام الله تعالى لا عين كلامه.
The linguists said: This speech is the speech of the Prophet, peace be upon him, so they attribute it to the first who spoke it. Likewise, when one of us recites the Qur'an, he says: This is the speech of Allah Almighty, meaning that Allah Almighty is the first who spoke it, and people recite it and follow it. Then whoever reflects on what I have required of him will know that this is not a separation from it because of what has passed, that this is not free from the three aspects according to how we divided and invalidated each division. Then his considering this analogously to what he considered from the speech of the Prophet, peace be upon him, is evidence that he believes that this is not the speech of Allah Almighty; rather, the speech of Allah Almighty existed then perished like the speech of the Prophet, peace be upon him, due to their being equal in his view in being accidents that cannot endure. And the intended meaning of their saying 'it is the speech of Allah Almighty' is that Allah Almighty is the one who preceded to it and spoke this utterance in this arrangement, then that ceased to exist, so the speaker speaks something like it, not the very same, as in the speech of the Prophet, peace be upon him. And this is the judgment regarding every poem, sermon, and letter: that the originator is the one who undertook that arrangement, then it went away, perished, and its attributes vanished. And whoever recites that poem or reads that sermon and letter recites and reads in that arrangement, so he becomes a speaker of something like what the first composer spoke, not that it is actually the speech of the first composer by consensus of the rational people, due to the impossibility of the endurance of the speech of Allah Almighty and of all speech, and due to what has been established as proof of the impossibility of saying that accidents endure. From this it follows that the recitation of the reciter should be, according to them, like the speech of Allah Almighty, not the very speech.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage presents a linguistic and theological argument about the attribution of speech to its original speaker. Nasafi begins by citing the common linguistic practice where people attribute a statement to the first person who uttered it, such as saying 'this is the speech of the Prophet' even when recited by others. Similarly, when a Muslim recites the Qur'an, he says 'this is the speech of God' meaning God is the original speaker. Nasafi then argues that the opponent (likely the Mu'tazili or a Karrāmi) who denies the eternity of God's speech must hold that the Qur'an as we recite it is not actually God's speech but a created likeness, because they consider speech to be an accident that cannot endure. Nasafi critiques this by showing the absurd consequence: the opponent would have to say that God's speech existed and then perished, just like human speech. Then Nasafi explains the correct understanding: when we say 'this is the speech of God', we mean that God originated this utterance in this arrangement, and then it ceased to exist. The reciter then speaks something similar to it, not identical, just as a poet's poem is attributed to him even when recited by others. The recitation is 'like' the original, not the very same, because the original cannot endure. This conclusion he draws: the recitation of the Qur'an is like God's speech, not the very speech itself. The passage refutes the opponent's implication while affirming that the Qur'an is truly God's speech in the sense of origination.

Key Terms
كلام الله
عرض
بقاء الأعراض
النظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when we say 'this is the speech of God' about the recited Qur'an, we mean the recited words are identical in substance to God's eternal attribute.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that it means God originated the utterance; the reciter speaks something like it, not the very same.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does it mean when a reciter says 'this is the speech of God' about the Qur'an?
A) The reciter's utterance is exactly the same as God's eternal speech.
B) God is the first who spoke it, and the reciter speaks something like it.
C) The Qur'an is an accident that perishes immediately after God speaks.
D) The recitation is a created copy of God's speech.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi teaches that calling the Qur'an 'the speech of God' means God is the original speaker, not that the recited words are identical to an eternal attribute.

Refutation of the Unity of Speech: A Reductio ad Absurdum
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite view on the unity of speech via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite view that the same speech of God subsists in every recitation and written copy by showing it leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
If one speech is everywhere, it must both exist and not exist at once.

This passage is a powerful refutation of the Mu'tazilite theologians al-Iskafi and al-Jubba'i, who held that God's speech is a single entity that inheres in all recitations and written copies. Nasafi demonstrates that this view leads to absurd consequences, forcing the opponent into a position akin to sophistry. As you read, focus on how he builds his reductio.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to grasp the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the initial supposition
Notice that Nasafi starts by supposing the opponents' view is true: that the same speech subsists in many tongues and mushafs.
This is the premise he will attack.
3
Follow the reductio
Trace how he derives impossible consequences: the speech would have to come into existence without increase and cease without decrease, implying simultaneous existence and non-existence.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Consider the analogy
Nasafi then gives an analogy about a single blackness inhering in all black bodies. Reflect on how this strengthens his point.
The analogy shows the absurdity of universal inherence.
5
Formulate the conclusion
Conclude that Nasafi's refutation forces his opponents into the position of the Sophists, which is rationally untenable.
This is the final verdict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول للإسكافي والجبائي: إن كان القارئ إذا قرأ القرآن، فهذا كلام الله تعالى قام بهذا اللسان، ولو كتب في المصحف قام بذلك المصحف وهو متلو ومكتوب، وهو بعينه في أكثر من ألف ألف مصحف، وقائم بأكثر من ألف ألف لسان وهو شيء واحد، ويقرأ الإنسان بعدما لم يكن يقرأ فيوجب في لسانه، ويكتب في مصحف بعد أن لم يكن فيصير قائما به وهو بعينه في ألفي مصحف ببخارى وفي مثل ذلك بقيروان المغرب، وكذا في جميع ديار أمصار الإسلام وقراها، وهو بنفسه شيء متحد الذات ليس بمتعدد، ثم يوجد في مصحف يكتب الآن ولم يزدد ويعدم عن مصحف انعدم ولم ينتقص وينعدم عن واحد مع وجوده في مصاحف أخرى، فلم يلزم الاستحالة واتصافه بالوجود والعدم فلا أصح إذا من مذهب السوفسطائية ولسنا نظن أن مجنونا يرضى بنسبه مثل هذا القول إليه. أرأيت لو أن قائلا قال: إن جميع ما اتصف من الأجسام بالسواد اتصف بسواد واحد حل في المحال كلها، وكذا البياض وسائر الألوان، وكذا الحركات والسكنات، وكذا القدرة والإرادات، وكذا الجسم الواحد يوجد في حالة واحدة بالمشرق والمغرب، وفي ألف كورة ومكان. أفيلتفت إلى قوله ويضيع الوقت بالإصغاء إلى ما يخيله؟
Then we say to al-Iskafi and al-Jubba'i: If the reciter when he recites the Qur'an, this is the speech of Allah Almighty subsisting in this tongue, and if it is written in the mushaf it subsists in that mushaf, and it is recited and written, and it is the very same in more than a thousand thousand mushafs, and subsisting in more than a thousand thousand tongues while being one thing, and a person reads after he was not reading so he brings it into existence in his tongue, and it is written in a mushaf after it was not so it comes to subsist in it, and it is the very same in two thousand mushafs in Bukhara and likewise in Qayrawan of the Maghrib, and likewise in all the lands of the Islamic cities and villages, and it is in itself a single essence, not multiple, then it comes to exist in a mushaf written now and does not increase, and ceases to exist from a mushaf that disappears and does not decrease, and ceases to exist from one while existing in other mushafs. So does impossibility not follow, and its being described with existence and non-existence? Then there is no sounder position than the doctrine of the Sophists, and we do not think that a madman would be pleased to have such a statement attributed to him. Do you not see that if someone said: All bodies that are described with blackness are described with a single blackness that inheres in all of them, and likewise whiteness and the rest of the colors, and likewise movements and rests, and likewise abilities and wills, and likewise a single body exists at one time in the east and the west, and in a thousand regions and places? Would anyone pay attention to his statement and waste time listening to what he imagines?
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of the Mu'tazilite scholars al-Iskafi and al-Jubba'i, likely regarding their view on the nature of the Qur'an as created speech. Nasafi presents a reductio ad absurdum: if the very speech of God (the same entity) is present in each recitation and each written copy, then it would have to be one single thing existing in countless tongues and mushafs simultaneously. This leads to contradictions: it would have to come into existence in a new mushaf without increase, and cease to exist from a destroyed mushaf without decrease, and simultaneously exist and not exist at the same time. Nasafi argues this is sophistry and no rational person would accept it. He further makes an analogy: if one claimed that a single blackness inheres in all black bodies, or that a single body could be in many places, no one would listen. This shows the absurdity of the opponent's position. The passage aims to defend the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that the Qur'an is uncreated and its recitation is a created act that conveys the uncreated speech, but here he is attacking the Mu'tazilite position that the speech is a created accident that cannot be in multiple places.

Key Terms
الإسكافي
الجبائي
السوفسطائية
حلول
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Nasafi is supporting the view that speech is a single entity present in many places.
Correction: In fact, Nasafi is refuting that view by demonstrating its absurd consequences.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Nasafi draw from the supposition that the very speech of God is present in every recitation and mushaf?
A) This proves the Qur'an is uncreated and eternal.
B) This leads to the impossible consequence of a single thing existing and not existing simultaneously.
C) This is the correct position of the Salaf.
D) This shows the speech must be multiple.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi uses reductio ad absurdum to show that the unity of speech leads to sophistry, thus defending the orthodox position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever pause to think that the same words are being recited by millions across the world at this moment? This passage forces you to examine what you truly believe about the nature of God's speech. Are you content with a shallow understanding, or will you seek to know how it is that the Quran is truly the word of Allah? What concrete step will you take today to deepen that understanding?
?
Nasafi calls the opposing view the 'doctrine of the Sophists' – a position no sane person would accept. Yet many Muslims hold confused beliefs about Allah's attributes without realizing the contradictions. Have you ever accepted a belief about Allah that, upon reflection, leads to logical absurdity? What would it mean for you to test your own beliefs with the same rigorous logic Nasafi uses here? Will you let go of any comfortable but ill-founded notion about your Creator?
