The Pillar Analogy: Why Divine Reports Remain True Through Time
Demonstrative proof using pillar analogy for divine reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the pillar analogy illustrates that divine reports and knowledge are immutable while the objects of knowledge change.

Core Idea
Change is in the objects, not in God's knowledge or reports.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a powerful demonstrative argument against the objection that divine reports would change if they speak of things that pass from future to past. He distinguishes between the report itself and what it reports about, showing that only the latter undergoes temporal change. A simple analogy of a pillar and a moving observer makes this clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the claim about reports, then knowledge, then the pillar analogy.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three temporal states
The author describes three scenarios for the reported thing: future (does not exist yet), present (exists), and past (has passed). Observe how the report remains unchanged in each case.
3
Understand the parallel with knowledge
The author applies the same logic to God's knowledge: knowledge of a thing before its existence, during, and after does not change; only the known object changes.
This reinforces the immutability of divine attributes.
4
Reflect on the pillar analogy
Imagine a stationary pillar. A person's orientation relative to it changes (front, back, right, left), but the pillar itself remains unchanged. This illustrates that change is relative to the observer, not the object.
Apply this to the case of divine reports: the report is like the pillar, and temporal states are like the observer's positions.
5
Connect to the overall argument
This demonstrative answer refutes the objection that God's reports would change over time. The report stays true regardless of when its content occurs.
The author also mentions that previous doubts have been resolved elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجواب البرهاني أن يقال : إن إخبار الله تعالى إخبار عن المخبرات بأحوالهم وأوصافها ، فإذا لم يوجد مخبره وجب علينا القول بأنه خبر عنه أنه يكون وإذا وجد مخبره، وجب القول إنه خبر أنه ثابت، وإذا انعدم ومضى وجب القول إنه خبر عنه أنه كان والتغير على المخبر عنه لا على الخبر دليله ما تلونا من الآيات. وكذا علمه تعالى بالمعلومات، فإن علم الله تعالى بوجود آدم عليه السلام قبل وجوده علم بأنه يوجد، وبعدما وجد علم بأنه موجود و بعد انقراضه علم بأنه كان، وكذا في كل موجود على التغير ولا تغير على العلم عندنا، ولا على الذات الذي علم به عند المعتزلة، إنما التغير على المعلوم فكذا في الخبر، ونظيره في المشاهدات الأسطوانة المنصوبة إذا توجه إليها إنسان كانت قدامه، وإذا حول ظهره إليها كانت خلفه، وإذا حول يمينه إليها كانت عن يمينه، وإذا حول يساره إليها كانت عن يساره، ولا تغير عليها إنما التغير على هذا الإنسان. فكذا فيما نحن فيه. وقد مر حل الشبهة الخامسة في حل الشبهة الثالثة، وحل الشبهة السادسة في حل الشبهة الأولى (وقد تقصينا عن حل الشبهة العقلية بحمد الله تعالى).
Then the demonstrative answer is to say: Indeed, the report of God Almighty is a report about the reported things with their states and attributes. So if its reported thing does not exist, we must say that it is a report that it will be; if its reported thing exists, we must say that it is a report that it is; if it is absent and has passed, we must say that it is a report that it was. And change is upon the reported thing, not upon the report, as evidence from the verses we have recited. And likewise His knowledge, Exalted, of the known things. For God's knowledge of the existence of Adam, peace be upon him, before his existence is knowledge that he will exist; after he exists, it is knowledge that he exists; and after his extinction, it is knowledge that he was. And likewise for every existent with change. There is no change in knowledge according to us, nor in the essence by which He knows according to the Mu'tazila; rather change is in the known. So too with the report. An analogy in observable things is a standing pillar: when a person faces it, it is in front of him; when he turns his back to it, it is behind him; when he turns to his right, it is to his right; when he turns to his left, it is to his left. There is no change in it; rather change is in this person. So too in our case. And the solution of the fifth doubt has passed in the solution of the third doubt, and the solution of the sixth doubt in the solution of the first, and we have fully treated the rational doubts by the praise of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This is a demonstrative (burhani) answer. The author distinguishes between the report (khabar) and the reported thing (mukhbar), and between knowledge ('ilm) and the known (ma'lum). He argues that change occurs only in the object, not in the report or knowledge itself. He then provides an analogy: a pillar remains unchanged while a person's orientation relative to it changes. This illustrates that God's knowledge and reports are immutable; what changes is the temporal state of the created objects. The passage also notes that previous doubts have been resolved.

Misconception: Divine reports must change when the events they describe move from future to past.
Correction: Change occurs in the reported object, not in the report itself. The report remains true regardless of temporal state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you truly believe that God's knowledge of you—every moment—never changes, and that His decree about you is fixed? How does this belief affect your fear of His judgment and your hope for His mercy? Will you ever use your changing moods or circumstances as an excuse to doubt His unchanging truth?
?
The pillar analogy shows that when you change your position, the pillar appears different even though it is unchanged. In your relationship with God, have you sometimes thought that God was distant or angry because you felt far from Him, when in reality He is fixed and it is you who have moved? What step will you take today to 'turn back' and realign your orientation to His unchanging reality?
?
God's report about Adam before, during, and after his existence is true. What does this imply about God's knowledge of your own beginning, your current state, and your end? How should this shape your attitude towards your past mistakes and your future hopes—do you trust that His promise of forgiveness is as certain as His knowledge?

God's Report and Knowledge Remain Unchanged by Temporal Events
Nasafi proves that God's report and knowledge are not changed by the passage of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's report and knowledge remain unchanged despite changes in the objects they refer to, and how Nasafi uses logical reasoning and analogy to defend this doctrine.

Core Idea
God's knowledge and speech are unchanging; only the known objects change.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi addresses a key theological challenge: if God speaks about future events, does His speech change when those events occur? Nasafi demonstrates that change applies only to the reported objects, not to God's attributes of speech or knowledge. He grounds his argument in Quranic verses and an everyday analogy, defending the orthodox position against those who might infer imperfection in God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where opponents claim past events have passed. Nasafi poses a dilemma about Quranic verses reporting events.
Notice the dilemma forces opponents to either reject the Quran or accept its truth.
2
Follow the demonstrative answer
The passage explains how God's report relates to objects in three temporal states: before, during, and after existence. Identify the three cases.
Change is on the reported thing, not the report.
3
Apply the same logic to knowledge
Nasafi applies the same reasoning to God's knowledge, using Adam as an example. Observe how knowledge of Adam before, during, and after his life remains unchanged.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite view that knowledge changes.
4
Learn from the analogy
Read the pillar analogy about a standing pillar and a moving person. Understand how the pillar's relation changes but the pillar itself does not.
Analogize to God's report/knowledge: the object's relation to time changes, but the attribute does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: نعم كل ذلك قد مضى. قيل: أكذب هذه الآيات أم لا ؟ فإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا دليلهم وإن قالوا: نعم. كفروا . ثم (١) الجواب البرهاني أن يقال : إن (٢) إخبار الله تعالى إخبار عن المخبرات بأحوالهم وأوصافها ، فإذا لم يوجد مخبره وجب علينا القول بأنه خبر عنه أنه يكون وإذا وجد مخبره، وجب القول إنه خبر أنه ثابت، وإذا انعدم ومضى وجب القول إنه خبر عنه أنه كان والتغير على المخبر عنه لا على الخبر دليله ما تلونا من الآيات. وكذا علمه تعالى بالمعلومات، فإن علم الله تعالى بوجود آدم عليه السلام قبل وجوده علم بأنه يوجد، وبعدما وجد علم بأنه موجود و بعد انقراضه علم بأنه كان، وكذا في كل موجود على التغير ولا تغير على العلم عندنا، ولا على الذات الذي علم به عند المعتزلة، إنما التغير على المعلوم فكذا في الخبر، ونظيره (٣) في المشاهدات الأسطوانة المنصوبة إذا توجه إليها إنسان كانت قدامه، وإذا حول ظهره إليها كانت خلفه، وإذا حول يمينه إليها كانت عن يمينه، وإذا حول يساره إليها كانت عن يساره، ولا تغير عليها إنما التغير على هذا الإنسان. فكذا فيما نحن فيه.
If they say: 'Yes, all that has passed.' It is said: 'Do you declare these verses false or not?' If they say: 'No,' they invalidate their proof. If they say: 'Yes,' they disbelieve. Then the demonstrative answer is to say: Indeed, the information (khabar) of God Most High is information about the reported things (mukhbarat) regarding their states and attributes. So if its reported thing does not exist, we must say that it is news about it that it will be. And if its reported thing exists, we must say that it is news that it is established. And if it ceases and passes, we must say that it is news about it that it was. Change is upon the reported thing, not upon the report. Its evidence is the verses we have recited. Likewise, His knowledge Most High of known things. For God's knowledge of the existence of Adam, peace be upon him, before his existence is knowledge that he will exist; after he existed, knowledge that he exists; and after his extinction, knowledge that he was. And likewise for every existent, change occurs, but there is no change upon knowledge according to us, nor upon the essence by which He knows according to the Mu'tazila; change is only upon the known. So likewise in the report. Its analogy in observable things is a standing pillar: if a person faces it, it is in front of him; if he turns his back to it, it is behind him; if he turns his right side to it, it is to his right; if he turns his left side to it, it is to his left. There is no change upon it; change is only upon this person. Thus it is in our case.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's report (khabar) and knowledge (ilm) are not subject to change, even when the objects reported or known change over time. The author begins by presenting an objection from opponents: they affirm that past events have passed. He then poses a dilemma: do they declare the verses (that report future events) false or not? If they say no, their proof is invalidated; if they say yes, they disbelieve. The demonstrative answer follows: God's information is about the states of the reported things. When the reported thing does not yet exist, the report is that it will be; when it exists, the report is that it is; when it has passed, the report is that it was. Change occurs on the reported thing, not on the report. The same applies to God's knowledge: knowledge of Adam before his existence is knowledge that he will exist; after existence, knowledge that he exists; after extinction, knowledge that he was. Change is only on the known object, not on the knowledge or the divine essence (contra the Mu'tazila who posit change in the essence). The author then provides an analogy from observable experience: a standing pillar does not change when a person moves around it; only the person's relation to it changes. Similarly, God's report and knowledge remain unchanged; change is only in the temporal realities of the objects.

Correction: Nasafi shows that God's report and knowledge are immutable; only the reported objects and known things undergo change.

D) The report is that the event was.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of speech and knowledge are eternally unchanging; only the objects they refer to undergo temporal change.

?

What would it mean to let the unchanging truth of His speech anchor you instead?
?

How might this reality transform your reading of those stories from mere history into a living dialogue with the One who speaks them?

Interpreting 'Amr' in the Quran
Imam Nasafi explains the verse 'And the command of Allah is done' as referring to punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi interprets 'amr' in the verse as 'affairs' or 'punishment' rather than the divine attribute of speech.

Core Idea
The word 'amr' in the Quran can mean 'affair' or 'punishment', not always 'command as speech'.

Imam Nasafi addresses a potential misunderstanding about a Quranic verse. He clarifies that when Allah says 'And the command of Allah is done', the word 'amr' does not refer to His eternal speech, but to His decree or punishment. This is a key point in defending the uncreatedness of the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the poet's line and how it is used as evidence.
The poet's usage shows 'amr' can mean 'affairs'.
2
Identify the key term
Focus on the word 'amr'. What does it usually mean in theological discussions? How does Nasafi argue it means something different here?
3
Understand the theological stakes
Why is this interpretation important? If 'amr' here meant speech, it could imply the Quran is created. Nasafi's interpretation protects the belief that the Quran is uncreated.
4
Analyze the poetic evidence
Consider why Nasafi quotes a poet. What function does this serve in his argument?
Poetry is used as a linguistic witness to establish meaning.
5
Reflect and apply
Think of other Quranic verses where 'amr' might be interpreted similarly. How does this lesson affect your understanding of divine attributes?
This connects to broader discussions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتأويل قوله تعالى: وَكَانَ أَمْرُ اللهِ مَفْعُولًا قال الشاعر: فقلت لهـا أمـري إلى الله كله وإني إليه في الإياب لراجع أي شئوني وأفعالي، ولم يرد به أمره الذي هو كلامه. والله الموفق.
And the interpretation of His saying: 'And the command of Allah is done.' The poet said: 'So I said to her: my command is entirely to Allah, and I will return to Him.' Meaning my affairs and my actions, and He did not intend by it His command which is His speech. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage provides an interpretation of the Quranic verse 'And the command of Allah is done' (Quran 33:37). Imam Nasafi argues that 'amr' here does not refer to the divine attribute of speech (the eternal command), but rather to Allah's decree, punishment, or affairs. He supports this by quoting a poet who says 'my command is to Allah,' meaning my affairs and actions. Thus, the word 'amr' can mean 'affair' or 'punishment', not the eternal speech. This is a defensive argument against opponents who might use such verses to claim that the Quran (as speech) is subject to being 'done' or created.

Key Terms

تأويل (interpretation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'amr' in the verse always refers to the divine attribute of speech (the eternal command).
Correction: Imam Nasafi shows that 'amr' can mean 'affair' or 'punishment', as evidenced by Arabic usage, thus the verse does not imply that Allah's speech is created.

Checkpoint

B) Allah's affairs or punishment

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi shows that 'amr' in the verse means 'affair' or 'punishment', not the eternal speech, defending the uncreated Quran.

Are you using them, or are you relying on your own reading? Commit to one concrete step to deepen your understanding of a verse you think you already know.

The Quran as Uncreated Attribute
Imam Nasafi argues that the Quran is the uncreated attribute of speech, refuting alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that the Quran, as an attribute of Allah, is uncreated, while its physical expressions (letters and sounds) are created.

Core Idea
The Quran's eternal attribute is uncreated; its recited words are created.

This passage from Imam Nasafi clarifies a crucial theological distinction: the Quran, as the attribute of speech subsisting in Allah's essence, is eternal and uncreated. However, the physical letters and sounds that we recite are created. Nasafi critiques both the Mu'tazila and the Hanabila for blurring this distinction, offering a clear Maturidi position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words come through.
Notice the concise style and the use of key terms like 'صفة القائمة بالذات'.
2
Identify the three groups mentioned
Find the Mu'tazila, the Hanabila, and the Hashwiyya. Understand each group's position on the Quran as reported by Nasafi.
The Mu'tazila say the Quran is created; Hanabila say the letters and sounds are uncreated; Hashwiyya say their own recitation is uncreated.
3
Focus on the critique of the Hanabila
Read carefully why Nasafi dismisses the Hanabila view. He calls it 'manifest nonsense' (هذيان ظاهر). Ask why he rejects it.
The distinction: the attribute is uncreated, but the physical expression is created.
4
Reflect on the core distinction
Summarize the lesson: the eternal attribute of speech is uncreated, but the physical recitation (letters, sounds) is created. This is the Maturidi position.
This distinction avoids both the Mu'tazili error of making the attribute created and the Hashwiyya error of making the recitation uncreated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرف بهذه الدلائل أن القرآن غير مخلوق، وأعني به الصفة القائمة بالذات وهي الكلام. وما يدعيه المعتزلة حدوثه فهو محدث كما زعموا، ومساعدتنا إياهم على ذلك تغنيهم عن إقامة الدليل عليه. وبمعرفة حقيقة المذهب يتبين أنهم يتكلمون في المسألة في غير محال النزاع، وإنما ذلك دليل لهم على الحنابلة القائلين: إن كلام الله تعالى هو الحروف المؤلفة والأصوات المقطعة، وأنه حال في الألسنة والمصاحف والصدور وأنه مع هذا غير مخلوق. وكثير من الحشوية يساعدونهم ويقولون: لفظي بالقرآن غير مخلوق، فيجعلون قراءتهم غير مخلوقة، وهذا هذيان ظاهر.
And it is known by these evidences that the Quran is uncreated, and by it I mean the attribute subsisting in the essence, which is speech. And what the Mu'tazila claim of its temporality is temporally originated as they have alleged. And our agreeing with them on that would suffice them from establishing proof for it. And by knowing the reality of the doctrine, it becomes clear that they are speaking about the matter outside the point of contention. Rather, that is evidence for them against the Hanabila who say: Indeed, the speech of Allah, exalted is He, is the composed letters and segmented sounds, and that it inheres in tongues, mushafs, and chests, and that it is nevertheless uncreated. And many of the Hashwiyya agree with them and say: My utterance of the Quran is uncreated, thus making their recitation uncreated. And this is manifest nonsense.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument establishing that the Quran is uncreated. Imam Nasafi first states that the previous evidences prove the Quran is uncreated, clarifying that he means the attribute of speech that subsists in Allah's essence. Then he critiques the Mu'tazila who claim the Quran is created (hadith). He notes that if Muslims agreed with them on that claim, the Mu'tazila would not need to provide proof—but Muslims reject this. Next, he points out that the Mu'tazila are actually arguing against the Hanabila, who believe that Allah's speech consists of actual letters and sounds that exist in tongues, mushafs, and chests, yet are still uncreated. Imam Nasafi dismisses this view as misguided. He also criticizes the Hashwiyya who say 'my utterance of the Quran is uncreated,' thereby deifying their own recitation. He calls this 'manifest nonsense' (hudhyan zahir). Thus, his position is clear: the Quran as an attribute is uncreated, but the physical expressions (letters, sounds, recitations) are created.

Key Terms
صفة القائمة بالذات (attribute subsisting in essence)
مخلوق (created)
محدث (temporally originated)
حروف وأصوات (letters and sounds)
هذيان (nonsense)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that reciting the Quran (the physical words) is uncreated because the Quran itself is uncreated.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that only the attribute of speech in Allah's essence is uncreated; the recited letters and sounds are created.

Checkpoint
What is the position of the Hanabila regarding Allah's speech?
A) It is created
B) It is composed of letters and sounds that are uncreated
C) It is only an attribute without letters
D) It is the same as the recitation

Key Takeaway
The Quran as an eternal divine attribute is uncreated, but its physical recitation in letters and sounds is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever feel that your own voice and pronunciation are somehow divine? How does Nasafi's sharp distinction between the eternal attribute and your created recitation challenge that feeling? Will you now pause before each recitation to consciously remind yourself that you are uttering something created that points to the Uncreated?
?
Nasafi calls the belief that 'my utterance of the Quran is uncreated' manifest nonsense. Have you ever, even unconsciously, thought that your Quran recitation has a special uncreated status? If you are honest, what does that reveal about your need to 'claim' something from Allah's attribute as your own? Can you today repent of seeking honor by attributing eternity to your own actions?
?
Consider the Mu'tazila and the Hanabila: one says the Quran is fully created, the other says even the letters are uncreated. Both are wrong according to Nasafi. Which error are you more prone to—draining the Quran of its divine weight by treating it as ordinary speech, or idolizing its physical form? What one concrete step will you take this week to correct that imbalance in your heart?

The Uncreated Qur'an and the True Meaning of God's Command
Nasafi interprets the verse and proves the Qur'an's uncreatedness while refuting sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi distinguishes God's command (amr) as decree from His speech (kalam) and why the Qur'an as an attribute is uncreated, while refuting sectarian errors.

Core Idea
God's command in the verse means His decree, not His speech; the Qur'an is His uncreated attribute.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla tackles a common confusion: does the Qur'anic verse 'And the command of Allah is a decree' refer to God's eternal speech? Nasafi clarifies that 'amr' here means decree and punishment, not His attribute of speech. Then he states the correct position on the Qur'an and critiques the Mu'tazila, Hanabila, and Hashwiyya. Let's unpack his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Nasafi starts with a verse interpretation and moves to a doctrinal conclusion.
Look for the key terms like 'غير مخلوق' and 'صفة قائمة بالذات'.
2
Understand the verse interpretation
Nasafi uses a line of poetry to show that 'amr' can mean matters/affairs, not just speech. Here it refers to God's decree, not His attribute.
This lexical distinction prevents misinterpretation.
3
Grasp the uncreatedness of the Qur'an
Nasafi states that the Qur'an (as God's attribute of speech subsisting in His essence) is uncreated. This is the Maturidi position.
He explicitly means the attribute, not the recitation or written copies.
4
Follow the refutations of other sects
He critiques the Mu'tazila who claim the Qur'an is created, notes that agreement with them on that point is unnecessary, and then attacks the Hanabila who say the Qur'an is letters and sounds yet uncreated, and the Hashwiyya who say their utterance is uncreated.
The real disagreement is with the Hanabila, as they locate the attribute in physical things.
5
Identify the essential takeaway
The Qur'an as God's eternal speech is uncreated; any other interpretation that confuses God's command with His speech or attributes the uncreatedness to human recitation is mistaken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتأويل قوله تعالى: وَكَانَ أَمْرُ اللهِ مَفْعُولًا . قال الشاعر: فقلت لها أمري إلى الله كله وإني إليه في الإياب لراجع أي شئوني وأفعالي، ولم يرد به أمره الذي هو كلامه. والله الموفق. وعرف بهذه الدلائل أن القرآن غير مخلوق، وأعني به الصفة القائمة بالذات وهي الكلام. وما يدعيه المعتزلة حدوثه فهو محدث كما زعموا، ومساعدتنا إياهم على ذلك تغنيهم عن إقامة الدليل عليه. وبمعرفة حقيقة المذهب يتبين أنهم يتكلمون في المسألة في غير محال النزاع، وإنما ذلك دليل لهم على الحنابلة القائلين: إن كلام الله تعالى هو الحروف المؤلفة والأصوات المقطعة، وأنه حال فى الألسنة والمصاحف والصدور وأنه مع هذا غير مخلوق. وكثير من الحشوية يساعدونهم ويقولون: لفظي بالقرآن غير مخلوق، فيجعلون قراءتهم غير مخلوقة، وهذا هذيان ظاهر.
And the interpretation of His statement, the Exalted: 'And the command of Allah is a decree.' The poet said: 'I said to her: my affair is entirely to Allah, and I am to Him returning.' i.e., my concerns and actions. And He did not intend by it His command which is His speech. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And it is known through these proofs that the Qur'an is uncreated, and I mean by it the attribute subsisting in the Essence, which is speech. And what the Mu'tazila claim of its temporality is temporal as they alleged, and our agreement with them on that relieves them from establishing proof for it. And by knowing the reality of the madhhab, it becomes clear that they speak about the issue in other than the locus of dispute, and that is only a proof for them against the Hanabila who say that the speech of Allah, the Exalted, is the composed letters and articulated sounds, and that it resides in tongues, copies of the Qur'an, and hearts, and that despite this it is uncreated. And many of the Hashwiyya support them and say: my utterance of the Qur'an is uncreated, thus they make their recitation uncreated, and this is apparent raving.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage begins with an interpretation of the verse 'And the command of Allah is a decree' to show that 'amr' here means punishment and vengeance, not the speech attribute, as the poet used 'amri' (my affair) to mean his own concerns. This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding. Then Nasafi states that from these proofs it is known that the Qur'an is uncreated, meaning the attribute of speech subsisting in God's essence. He criticizes the Mu'tazila who claim the Qur'an is created, but notes that if Maturidis agreed with them on that point, it would obviate the need for proof. He shows that the real disagreement is with the Hanabila who say the Qur'an is composed of letters and sounds yet still uncreated. Nasafi dismisses the Hashwiyya who say their own recitation is uncreated as clear nonsense.

Misconception: Some think that the verse 'And the command of Allah is a decree' refers to God's speech (kalam) rather than His decree.
Correction: Nasafi shows that 'amr' here means punishment and affairs, not speech, using linguistic evidence.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an as God's eternal attribute of speech is uncreated; confusing it with His decree or with created letters and sounds is a serious error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Nasafi calls the belief that one's utterance of the Qur'an is uncreated 'apparent raving'. Have you ever, knowingly or unknowingly, held a similar feeling of false reverence—treating the physical mushaf or your own recitation as if it were divine? What would it cost you to humble yourself and acknowledge that only Allah's attribute is uncreated, while everything else, including your worship, is created and contingent?
?
If the Qur'an is truly the uncreated speech of Allah, then every verse you read is a direct address from your eternal Lord. Are you living as though that is true? Or do you reduce the Qur'an to a book of guidance you consult when convenient? What would change in your life today if you truly believed that the words you recite have always been with Allah and will never pass away?

The Maturidi Response to 'Hikaya' Objection
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila objection that 'hikaya' implies similarity between created recitation and divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Maturidi positions regarding the term 'hikaya' for the Quran and why they reject the Mu'tazila claim that it implies creation.

Core Idea
The term 'hikaya' does not necessarily imply similarity; it is a linguistic distinction that safeguards divine speech.

This passage addresses a classic theological objection: if we call the recited Quran a 'hikaya' (narrative) of God's speech, does that not imply the recitation is a copy, thus created? Al-Nasafi unpacks the Maturidi response, showing that the term itself is debated among scholars and even if used, it does not entail similarity. The core of the argument is a precise linguistic analysis of 'hikaya' versus 'muhakah'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Identify the objection: the Mu'tazila claim that calling the Quran a 'hikaya' of God's speech implies exact similitude, which would make God's speech created.
Notice the logical structure: if A is a narrative of B, then A is similar to B, and A is created, so B must be created.
2
Examine the two Maturidi responses
The first response: some Maturidis avoid the term 'hikaya' altogether, so the objection does not apply. The second response: those who use the term deny that it implies similarity.
The second response is more elaborate and is the focus of the passage.
3
Analyze the evidence for 'hikaya' not implying similarity
The author gives two proofs: (1) a translator narrates in different words/languages without similarity, (2) 'hakā' (narrate) is distinct from 'hākā' (imitate).
This linguistic distinction is key to the Maturidi defense.
4
Understand the conclusion and cautious practice
The disagreement is terminological, not doctrinal. As a precaution, many scholars avoid 'hikaya' and instead say 'qāla Allāhu khabaran 'an fulān'.
This shows the practical outcome of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة يوردون على أصحابنا سؤالا فيقولون: هذه الألفاظ حكاية عن كلامه، والحكاية تقتضي المماثلة وهذه الألفاظ والعبارات محدثة فكذلك المحكي عنه أو يقال : إن هذه الألفاظ لما كانت من جنس الحروف والأصوات كان المحكي عنه هكذا.
فيقال لهم: من منع من أصحابنا إطلاق لفظة الحكاية وهم المتأخرون الآخذون بالجزم فلا سؤال عليهم. ومن أجاز الإطلاق فهو يمنع المماثلة ويقول: الحكاية لا تقتضي المماثلة ولا تقتضي إلا ما تقتضيه الإخبار والعبارة والأداء. يحققه أن الترجمان يعبر بعبارة سوى الأولى، وبلسان سوى الأول، وتسمى حكاية ولا مشابهة بين الحكاية والمحكي يدل عليه أن من أخبر عن كلام غيره، يقال: حكي كلامه، ولا يقال حاكى فلان فلانا إلا إذا أتى بمثل حركاته ونغمته قصدا منه للتشبه به، وبدون ذلك لا يقال حكي عنه، ولو كانت الحكاية تقتضي المماثلة كالمحاكاة لما امتنعوا عن ذلك. والحاصل أن أصحابنا يأبون القول بثبوت المماثلة بين كلام الله تعالى وبين هذه العبارات واختلفوا بعد ذلك في معنى الحكاية لغةً، فإن ثبت أنها تقتضي المماثلة امتنع الكل، وإن ثبت أنها لا تقتضي المماثلة جوز الكل الإطلاق، فإذا هذا اختلاف العبارة دون المعنى، ولا عبرة له، والاحتياط الامتناع عند وقوع الاختلاف، ولهذا يختار ذو الاحتياط من مشايخنا أن يقولوا قال الله تعالى خبرا عن فلان، ولا يقولون : حكاية فلان وبالله التوفيق.
And the Mu'tazila raise an objection against our companions, saying: These words (al-alfāẓ) are a narrative (ḥikāya) of His speech, and narrative necessitates similarity (al-mumāthala), and these words and expressions are created, so likewise the narrated (al-maḥkī) is created. Or it is said: Since these words are of the kind of letters and sounds, the narrated must be like this. So it is said to them: Whoever among our companions forbids the use of the term 'narrative' (ḥikāya)—and they are the later ones who adhere to certainty—then no objection applies to them. And whoever permits its use, he denies similarity and says: Narrative does not necessitate similarity, nor does it necessitate anything other than what report (ikhbār), expression (ʿibāra), and conveyance (adāʾ) necessitate. This is confirmed by the fact that the translator (tujumān) expresses with a different expression from the first, and in a different language from the first, and it is called a narrative, yet there is no similarity between the narrative and the narrated. Evidence for this is that whoever reports the speech of another, it is said: 'He narrated (ḥakā) his speech,' and it is not said: 'He imitated (ḥākā) so-and-so' unless he performs like his movements and intonation intentionally to resemble him, and without that it is not said: 'He narrated' (ḥukiya) about him. And if narrative required similarity like imitation (muḥākāh), they would not have refrained from that. The conclusion is that our companions reject the assertion of similarity between the speech of God Most High and these expressions. And they differed afterward about the meaning of 'narrative' linguistically. If it is established that it necessitates similarity, then all refrain [from using the term]; if it is established that it does not necessitate similarity, then all permit its use. So this is a difference of expression, not of meaning, and there is no weight to it. And caution is to refrain when disagreement occurs. For this reason, the cautious among our shaykhs choose to say: 'God Most High said, informing about so-and-so,' and they do not say: 'narrative of so-and-so.' And with God is success.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The Mu'tazila object to the Maturidi use of the term 'hikaya' for the recited Quran, arguing that 'hikaya' implies exact similitude (mumathala) between the created recitation and God's eternal speech, thus making the Quran created. Al-Nasafi first points out that some later Maturidis (al-muta'akhkhirun) avoid the term 'hikaya' altogether, so the objection does not affect them. For those who permit the term, they deny that 'hikaya' necessarily implies similarity. The author provides two proofs: (1) A translator can express the same meaning in different words and languages, and this is called 'hikaya' without any similarity of form. (2) Linguistically, 'hakā' (to narrate) is distinct from 'hākā' (to imitate). One can narrate someone's speech without mimicking their tone or gestures. If 'hikaya' required similarity, it would be the same as 'muhakah' (imitation). The conclusion is that the disagreement is merely terminological, not doctrinal. As a precaution, the preferred practice is to avoid saying 'hikaya' and instead say 'qāla Allāhu khabaran 'an fulān' (God said, informing about so-and-so).

Key Terms
حكاية
مماثلة
إخبار
عبارة
ترجمان
محاكاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Maturidi scholars accept the term 'hikaya' for the Quran and believe it implies similarity.
Correction: The passage shows that some Maturidis forbid the term entirely, and those who permit it explicitly deny that it implies similarity. The term is avoided by cautious scholars as a precaution.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some Maturidi scholars avoid the term 'hikaya' for the Quran?
A) Because they believe the Quran is uncreated and 'hikaya' would imply creation.
B) Because they hold that 'hikaya' necessarily implies exact similarity, which would be problematic if applied to God's speech.
C) Because they reject all terms used by the Mu'tazila.
D) Because they think the Quran is not a narrative of God's speech.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi response defends the uncreatedness of God's speech by showing that 'hikaya' does not imply similarity, and that terminological caution is prudent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever reflect that the words you are uttering are not the same as God's eternal speech, yet they carry its exact meaning? How does that awareness change the way you recite?
?
Are there theological terms you use carelessly without understanding their implications? How can you adopt the cautious, precise approach of the Maturidi scholars in your own speech about Allah?
?
This lesson shows that scholars may differ in terminology but agree on core belief. When you encounter disagreements among Muslims today, do you focus on differences of expression or do you seek the underlying truth? Which approach would bring you closer to Allah?

Three Views on What is Heard in the Quran
Al-Nasafi enumerates three opinions on what is heard when hearing the Quran and critiques the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three different opinions on what is perceived when hearing the Quran and why al-Nasafi refutes the first two.

Core Idea
The debate over what is heard when hearing the Quran touches the nature of God's speech and the limits of sense perception.

Al-Nasafi presents three competing views about what a person actually hears when they listen to the Quran. These views reflect deeper theological disagreements about whether divine attributes can be perceived by the senses. By examining them, we see how different theological principles lead to different conclusions about the same experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own presentation of the three views come through.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three opinions
Find each opinion and note who it is attributed to. The first is from Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, the second from al-Ash'ari, and the third is unnamed but supported by a Quranic verse.
The third opinion appears to be al-Nasafi's preferred view.
3
Understand the refutation
Notice how al-Nasafi critiques the first opinion, calling it 'close to denying realities' and arguing that it leads to an impossibility. What is the logical flaw he points out?
He says if hearing the Quran means hearing God's essence, then anyone who hears the Quran would know God's essence through hearing, which is impossible.
4
Connect to broader principles
See how each view stems from a principle about perception and accidents. Al-Qattan denies that accidents can be perceived; al-Ash'ari holds that every existent can be perceived; the third view distinguishes between sound and speech.
This shows how foundational assumptions lead to different theological conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف الناس في المسموع، حكي عن عبد الله بن سعيد القطان أن المسموع هو ذات المتكلم لا الكلام، وذات ذي الصوت لا الصوت جريا منه على أصله إذ شيئا من الأعراض والصفات لا تعرف بالحواس، ولهذا لم يعد نفاة الأعراض من جملة منكري المحسوسات، فعلى هذا أن من سمع كلام الله فقد ذاته، فيكون ذاته مسموعا، وهذا قريب من إنكار الحقائق، لأن كون الصوت مسموعا حقيقة، وهو أيضا دعوى ما يعرف بطلانه بالبداية، فإن هذا يقتضي أن من سمع كلام الله عرف ثبوت ذاته بحاسة السمع وهذا محال.
ومنهم من قال : كل موجود يصح أن يسمع وهو المحكي عن الأشعري جريا على أصله أن كل موجود يصح أن يرى. ومنهم من قال: المسموع شيئان الصوت والكلام، وكل سامع القرآن من قارئ القرآن يسمع عنده كلام الله تعالى من الله تعالى قال الله تعالى: وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَمَ اللَّهِ.
The people differed concerning what is heard (al-masmūʿ). It is narrated from ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd al-Qaṭṭān that what is heard is the essence (dhāt) of the speaker, not the speech, and the essence of the one with the voice, not the voice, following his principle that none of the accidents (aʿrāḍ) and attributes (ṣifāt) are known by the senses. For this reason, the deniers of accidents are not counted among the deniers of perceptibles. Accordingly, whoever hears the speech of God has heard His essence, so His essence becomes heard. This is close to denying realities, because the sound being heard is a reality. And it is also a claim whose falsehood is known at the outset, for this would require that whoever hears the speech of God knows the existence of His essence through the sense of hearing, and this is impossible. And among them is one who said: Every existing thing can be heard—this is narrated from al-Ashʿarī, following his principle that every existing thing can be seen. And among them is one who said: What is heard are two things: the sound and the speech. And every hearer of the Quran from a reciter of the Quran hears, in his view, the speech of God Most High from God Most High. God Most High said: 'And if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the word of Allah.'
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This is a classification passage presenting three opinions about what is heard when one hears the Quran. The first opinion, attributed to Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, claims that what is heard is the essence of the speaker, not the speech or sound. This is based on his principle that accidents and attributes cannot be perceived by the senses, so the only thing that can be heard is the substance itself. Al-Nasafi refutes this as absurd because it would mean that anyone hearing the Quran directly perceives God's essence, which is impossible by sense of hearing. The second opinion, from al-Ash'ari, holds that every existing thing can be heard, consistent with his view that every existing thing can be seen. The third opinion, which al-Nasafi seems to adopt, states that two things are heard: the sound (of the reciter) and the divine speech (kalam Allah). He supports this with the verse where God says 'so that he may hear the word of Allah' (Q 9:6), implying that the Prophet's contemporaries heard the speech of God when the Quran was recited to them.

Key Terms
مسموع
ذات
صوت
كلام
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ashʿarī's view means God's speech is a physical sound.
Correction: The passage only states that al-Ashʿarī holds every existent can be heard, not that divine speech is a sound. His principle is about perception in general.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason al-Nasafi rejects the view of Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan?
A) Because it denies that accidents exist.
B) Because it implies that hearing the Quran means hearing God's essence, which is impossible.
C) Because it contradicts the Quranic verse about hearing the word of Allah.
D) Because it claims that the sound is not heard.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi distinguishes between the sound of the reciter and the divine speech, both of which are heard when listening to the Quran, avoiding the extremes of denying sense perception or equating God's essence with audible sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever pause to consider what exactly you are hearing—the sound of your own voice or the speech of God? How does distinguishing between the two change the reverence with which you listen?
?
Al-Nasafi calls the first view 'close to denying realities.' Are there areas in your own faith where you have dismissed clear sensory or scriptural evidence in favor of an abstract theological preference? What would it cost you to accept what is plainly true?
?
The third view affirms that you hear both sound and divine speech. In your next recitation, will you consciously strive to direct your heart beyond the melody to the Word itself? What concrete step will you take to make this shift permanent?

Differing Views on What Is Heard (al-Masmū‘)
Nasafi reports and critiques views on hearing God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different theological positions on what is heard when God's speech is involved, and the critiques of each position presented by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Hearing God's speech does not entail hearing His essence or any sensible attribute.

In this passage, al-Nasafi surveys several scholarly opinions on the nature of what is heard (al-masmū‘) when it comes to divine speech. The discussion is critical for understanding how Muslims conceive of hearing God's word—whether it is the sound, the speech itself, or even God's essence. Let's explore these views and their critiques.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the arguments.
Pay attention to the different positions introduced.
2
Identify the first two views
Note al-Qaṭṭān's view that what is heard is the speaker's essence, and al-Ash‘arī's view that every existent can be heard.
Consider why al-Nasafi finds the first view impossible.
3
Examine the third view and its critique
Understand Ibn Fūrak's position that both sound and speech are heard, and how al-Nasafi refutes it using the Mu‘tazila argument.
The critique hinges on the sensible nature of sound.
4
Grasp al-Māturīdī's position
Notice that al-Māturīdī allows hearing beyond sound but does not agree with Ibn Fūrak's claim of hearing God's speech from a reciter.
This shows a nuanced stance within the Maturidi school.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف الناس في المسموع، حكي عن عبد الله بن سعيد القطان أن المسموع هو ذات المتكلم لا الكلام، وذات ذي الصوت لا الصوت جريا منه على أصله إذ شيئا من الأعراض والصفات لا تعرف بالحواس، ولهذا لم يعد نفاة الأعراض من جملة منكري المحسوسات، فعلى هذا أن من سمع كلام الله فقد ذاته، فيكون ذاته مسموعا، وهذا قريب من إنكار الحقائق، لأن كون الصوت مسموعا حقيقة، وهو أيضا دعوى ما يعرف بطلانه بالبداية، فإن هذا يقتضي أن من سمع كلام الله عرف ثبوت ذاته بحاسة السمع وهذا محال. ومنهم من قال : كل موجود يصح أن يسمع وهو المحكي عن الأشعري جريا على أصله أن كل موجود يصح أن يرى . ومنهم من قال: المسموع شيئان الصوت والكلام ، وكل سامع القرآن من قارئ القرآن يسمع عنده كلام الله تعالى من الله تعالى قال الله تعالى: وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَمَ اللَّهِ، وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَقَدْ كَانَ فَرِيقٌ مِّنْهُمْ يَسْمَعُونَ كَلَمَ اللَّهِ ﴾ عليه وموسى السلام سمع كلام الله تعالى من غير واسطة صوت وقراءة. وإلى هذا القول ذهب أبو بكر محمد بن الحسن بن فورك الأصبهاني من جملة الأشعرية قال: وإلى هذا أشار الأشعري حيث قال: سمع موسى عليه السلام ربه متكلما، وظاهر هذا يقتضي أنه سمع كلام ربه كما يقال : وجدت أباك عالما وأخاك عاقلا، أي: وجدت علم أبيك وعقل أخيك قال تعالى: ﴿ لَوَجَدُوا اللَّهَ تَوَّابًا رَحِيمًا ﴾ وقال تعالى: ثُمَّ يَسْتَغْفِرِ اللَّهَ يَجِدِ اللَّهَ غَفُورًا رَحِيمًا أي: يجد منه تعالى الرحمة وقبول التوبة، ويجد منه الرحمة والمغفرة بعد الاستغفار . وقد أشار الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي في أول مسألة الصفات من كتاب التوحيد إلى جواز سماع ما وراء الصوت فإنه قال : العلم بالأصوات وخفيات سمعا وخفيات الضمير هي الكلام في الشاهد عنده، فجوز سماع ما ليس بصوت إلا أنه لا يقول إنه يسمع كلام الله عند سماع قراءة القارئ إنما قال ذلك ابن فورك، وهذا القول أيضا مما لا تعويل عليه، إذ لو سمع عند قراءة القارئ كلام الله من الله لصار كلامه تعالى ثابتا بالحس ولما أنكرت المعتزلة كلامه القائم بذاته يعد سماعهم إياه، ولو أنكروا لنسبوا إلى العناد والمكابرة. وقال أبو العباس القلانسي : كلام الله تعالى مسموع من الله تعالى، وهو
People differed concerning what is heard. It is related from Abdullah ibn Sa‘id al-Qaṭṭān that what is heard is the essence of the speaker, not the speech, and the essence of the one possessing the sound, not the sound, proceeding from his principle that nothing of accidents and attributes is known by the senses. For this reason, those who deny accidents are not counted among the deniers of sensibles. According to this, whoever hears the speech of God has heard His essence, so His essence is audible. This is close to denying realities, because sound being audible is a reality, and it is also a claim whose falsity is known by intuition, for this would entail that whoever hears the speech of God knows the existence of His essence by the sense of hearing, and this is impossible. And among them is he who said: every existent can be heard, and this is related from al-Ash‘arī, proceeding from his principle that every existent can be seen. And among them is he who said: what is heard is two things: sound and speech, and every hearer of the Qur’an from a reciter of the Qur’an hears at that time the speech of God Almighty from God Almighty. God Almighty said: {And if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the word of Allah}. And He said: {And there was a party among them who used to hear the word of Allah}. And upon him and Moses, peace be upon him, heard the speech of God Almighty without an intermediary of sound and recitation. To this view went Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Fūrak al-Iṣbahānī among the Ash‘arites. He said: al-Ash‘arī indicated this when he said: Moses, peace be upon him, heard his Lord speaking. The apparent meaning of this entails that he heard the speech of his Lord, just as it is said: you found your father learned and your brother intelligent, meaning: you found your father's learning and your brother's intelligence. God Almighty said: {they would have found Allah Forgiving and Merciful}. And He said: {then seeks forgiveness of Allah, he will find Allah Forgiving and Merciful}, meaning: he finds from Him mercy and acceptance of repentance, and finds from Him mercy and forgiveness after seeking forgiveness. And the Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Māturīdī indicated at the beginning of the chapter on attributes in the book al-Tawḥīd the permissibility of hearing beyond sound, for he said: knowledge of sounds and hidden things audibly and hidden things of the inner self is speech in the witnessed in his view. So he permitted hearing what is not sound, except that he does not say that one hears the speech of God when hearing the recitation of the reciter; rather, Ibn Fūrak said that. And this view is also not to be relied upon, because if when hearing the recitation of the reciter one heard the speech of God from God, then His speech would be established by sense, and the Mu‘tazila would not have denied His speech subsisting in His essence despite their hearing it, and if they denied it they would be accused of obstinacy and contrariness. And Abu al-‘Abbās al-Qalānisī said: the speech of God Almighty is heard from God Almighty, and it is
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage classifies and critiques different theological views on what is heard (al-masmū‘) regarding God's speech. It begins by reporting the view of Abdullah ibn Sa‘id al-Qaṭṭān, who holds that what is heard is the essence of the speaker, not the speech itself, based on his principle that accidents and attributes are not known by the senses. The author then criticizes this view as tantamount to denying realities, since sound is audibly a reality, and it would absurdly imply that hearing God's speech gives knowledge of God's essence by hearing, which is impossible. Next, the view of al-Ash‘arī is mentioned, that every existent can be heard, based on his analogy with the permissibility of seeing every existent. Then a third view is presented: what is heard is two things, the sound and the speech, and when one hears the Qur'an from a reciter, he hears God's speech from God. This view is supported by Quranic verses about hearing God's word. The author notes that Ibn Fūrak adopted this view and cited al-Ash‘arī's statement that Moses heard his Lord speaking, interpreting it as hearing His speech, analogous to finding someone's learning. The passage then turns to al-Māturīdī's position: he allows hearing what is beyond sound, but he does not say that one hears God's speech at the time of hearing the reciter's recitation; that is Ibn Fūrak's claim. The author dismisses Ibn Fūrak's view as unreliable, arguing that if God's speech were heard from a reciter, it would be established by sense, and the Mu‘tazila would not have denied it—they would be obstinate if they did. Finally, the view of Abu al-‘Abbās al-Qalānisī is introduced: God's speech is heard from God, but the text cuts off.

Correction: The passage shows that hearing God's speech is not sensory perception of His essence; sound is a reality, but God's speech is not sound.

D) It is not supported by any Sunni scholar.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that God's speech is heard from God, but not from a reciter; hearing it does not entail hearing God's essence or any sensible attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage warns against the absurdity of claiming to perceive God’s essence through hearing. Ibn Furak's View on Hearing Allah's Speech and Its Refutation
Ibn Furak's view and its refutation via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Furak's claim that Allah's speech is heard during Quran recitation, and the Maturidi refutation showing its absurdity.

Core Idea
If Allah's speech were heard, everyone would sense it, including the Mu'tazila.

In this passage, the author first presents the view of Ibn Furak, an Ash'ari scholar who claimed that when someone recites the Quran, the listener hears both the reciter's voice and Allah's speech itself. Ibn Furak supports his position with an interpretation of al-Ash'ari's statement about Prophet Musa hearing his Lord speaking. However, the author refutes this view by showing that if Allah's speech were sensorily perceived, even the Mu'tazila—who deny the eternal speech—would have to acknowledge it, leading to an absurd conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Find Ibn Furak's claim
Read the passage and identify the main claim: that when a reciter recites, the listener hears both the voice and the speech of Allah.
Ibn Furak is an Ash'ari scholar.
2
Examine the supporting evidence
Note how Ibn Furak uses al-Ash'ari's statement about Musa and Quranic analogies to justify his view.
The analogies involve finding someone's attributes through them.
3
See al-Maturidi's position
Al-Maturidi allowed hearing what is not a sound but did not affirm hearing Allah's speech at recitation—only Ibn Furak did.
4
Understand the refutation
The author argues that if Allah's speech were heard during recitation, it would be a sensory object, which even the Mu'tazila would have to admit; their denial would be obstinacy, proving the view false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى هذا القول ذهب أبو بكر محمد بن الحسن بن فورك الأصبهاني من جملة الأشعرية قال: وإلى هذا أشار الأشعري حيث قال: سمع موسى عليه السلام ربه متكلما، وظاهر هذا يقتضي أنه سمع كلام ربه كما يقال : وجدت أباك عالما وأخاك عاقلا، أي: وجدت علم أبيك وعقل أخيك قال تعالى: ﴿ لَوَجَدُوا اللَّهَ تَوَّابًا رَحِيمًا ﴾ وقال تعالى: ثُمَّ يَسْتَغْفِرِ اللَّهَ يَجِدِ اللَّهَ غَفُورًا رَحِيمًا أي: يجد منه تعالى الرحمة وقبول التوبة، ويجد منه الرحمة والمغفرة بعد الاستغفار. وقد أشار الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي في أول مسألة الصفات من كتاب التوحيد إلى جواز سماع ما وراء الصوت فإنه قال : العلم بالأصوات وخفيات سمعا وخفيات الضمير هي الكلام في الشاهد عنده، فجوز سماع ما ليس بصوت إلا أنه لا يقول إنه يسمع كلام الله عند سماع قراءة القارئ إنما قال ذلك ابن فورك، وهذا القول أيضا مما لا تعويل عليه، إذ لو سمع عند قراءة القارئ كلام الله من الله لصار كلامه تعالى ثابتا بالحس ولما أنكرت المعتزلة كلامه القائم بذاته يعد سماعهم إياه، ولو أنكروا لنسبوا إلى العناد والمكابرة.
Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Furak al-Isbahani, among the Ash'aris, adopted this view, saying: al-Ash'ari alluded to this when he said: Musa, peace be upon him, heard his Lord speaking, and the apparent meaning of this requires that he heard the speech of his Lord, just as one says: 'I found your father knowledgeable and your brother intelligent,' i.e., I found your father's knowledge and your brother's intellect. Allah the Exalted said: 'They would have found Allah Oft-Returning, Merciful,' and He said: 'Then he seeks forgiveness from Allah, he finds Allah Oft-Forgiving, Merciful,' i.e., he finds from Him mercy and acceptance of repentance, and he finds mercy and forgiveness after seeking forgiveness. And Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi indicated at the beginning of the chapter on attributes in the Book of Tawhid the permissibility of hearing what is beyond sound, for he said: Knowledge of sounds and hidden hearings and hidden intentions is speech in the witnessed realm according to him, so he permitted hearing what is not a sound, except that he does not say that one hears the speech of Allah when hearing a reciter's recitation; only Ibn Furak said that. And this view too is unreliable, for if at a reciter's recitation one heard the speech of Allah from Allah, then His speech would become established by sensation, and the Mu'tazila would not have denied His speech subsisting in His essence after hearing it, and if they denied it, they would be accused of obstinacy and stubbornness.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Ibn Furak, an Ash'ari scholar, and then refutes it. It begins by stating that Ibn Furak claimed that when a reciter recites the Quran, one hears both the reciter's voice and the speech of Allah Himself. He supports this by interpreting al-Ash'ari's statement that Musa heard his Lord speaking as meaning he heard the speech of His Lord, not just a sound. He provides linguistic analogies from the Quran where finding someone implies finding their attributes. The author then notes that al-Maturidi, in his Kitab al-Tawhid, allowed hearing what is not a sound but did not assert that one hears Allah's speech during recitation; only Ibn Furak said that. The refutation follows: if Allah's speech were heard at recitation, it would be perceptible by the senses, and the Mu'tazila, who deny the eternal speech attribute, would have to acknowledge it after hearing it; if they still denied it, they would be guilty of sheer obstinacy. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum showing the inconsistency of Ibn Furak's position.

Key Takeaway

What will you do to remind yourself that the recitation is only a sound indicating something far greater?

Al-Qalanisi on Hearing Allah's Speech
Al-Qalanisi's position that Allah's speech is heard without being an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qalanisi's distinctive position that Allah's speech is heard directly from Allah, yet not as a created sound (accident), and how it differs from ordinary auditory perception.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is heard, but not as a created sound.

Al-Qalanisi offers a nuanced middle position: he affirms that Allah's speech is heard, but he denies that hearing is the perception of a created accident. This short passage presents his view and situates it alongside that of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: كلام الله (speech of Allah), مسموع (heard), and الأعراض (accidents).
2
Identify al-Qalanisi's core claim
Identify the two parts of his claim: (1) Allah's speech is heard from Allah, and (2) hearing does not perceive any accident. Why does he need to add the second part?
This distinguishes his view from those who say speech is a created sound.
3
Connect with al-Qattan's doctrine
The passage says this is the 'doctrine of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan.' What does this comparison tell you about the broader theological landscape?
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why this distinction matters for the doctrine of divine attributes. If Allah's speech is not an accident, what does that say about its nature?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو العباس القلانسي : كلام الله تعالى مسموع من الله تعالى، وهو يأبى وقوع الحس على شيء من الأعراض كما هو مذهب عبد الله بن سعيد القطان وجوزوا وقوع السمع على كلام الله تعالى.
And Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi said: The speech of Allah the Exalted is heard from Allah the Exalted, and it refuses the occurrence of sense perception upon any of the accidents, just as is the doctrine of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, and they permitted the occurrence of hearing upon the speech of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This short passage reports a second position, that of al-Qalanisi. He affirms that Allah's speech is heard, but not as a created sound. Rather, hearing occurs directly from Allah, but not in the ordinary way that sense perception falls upon accidents (created qualities). This is similar to the view of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. The key nuance is that al-Qalanisi and his followers consider Allah's speech to be an attribute that is heard, but hearing does not perceive it as a created sound; it is a special kind of hearing that relates to the divine attribute itself.

Correction: Al-Qalanisi rejects that hearing falls upon accidents; thus, hearing Allah's speech does not require it to be a created sound.

Checkpoint

B) Hearing occurs from Allah but not upon accidents.
C) Hearing is impossible for anyone.
D) Only prophets hear it.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qalanisi affirms that Allah's speech is heard directly from Allah, but he insists that hearing does not occur upon accidents, meaning it is not a created sound.

?
The passage distinguishes between ordinary hearing (of created sounds) and a special hearing of Allah's speech. Have you ever experienced a moment when you felt you were hearing the Qur'an not as mere sound but as a direct address from Allah? If not, what barriers in your heart might be preventing that? Ask Allah to remove them.

Al-Baqillani's View on Hearing Allah's Speech
Al-Baqillani's view on the audibility of Allah's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's position that Allah's speech is not ordinarily heard but can be heard miraculously, and how this reconciles divine transcendence with prophetic experiences.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is transcendent but can be heard by miracle.

This lesson presents the view of Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, a prominent Ash'ari theologian, on the hearing of Allah's speech. He distinguishes the ordinary recitation of the Qur'an from the divine speech itself, allowing that Allah may make His speech audible by extraordinary means. This position accounts for the experiences of prophets and the believers in the afterlife while preserving the uncreated nature of the divine word.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the key phrase 'على العادة الجارية' indicating the usual custom.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify what al-Baqillani says about the usual custom and the exception. How does he define the ordinary hearing of Allah's speech?
He says it is not heard ordinarily; only the reciter's voice is heard.
3
Understand the examples
Examine the examples provided: Musa on the mount, Muhammad on the Night of Ascension, and believers in the Hereafter. What do these show?
They show that hearing Allah's speech is possible by miraculous overturning of custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال: إن كلام الله تعالى ليس بمسموع على العادة الجارية بل سمع صوت القارئ فحسب، ولكن من الجائز أن يسمع على قلب العادة الجارية كما موسى عليه السلام على الطور ومحمد الله ليلة المعراج على طريقة الكرامة، وقد يسمع المؤمنون ذلك أيضا في الآخرة. وإليه يذهب أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب الباقلاني من جملة الأشعرية.

And to this goes Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Baqillani among the Ash'aris.

Commentary
This passage presents the third position, attributed to al-Baqillani and some Ash'aris. It states that Allah's speech is not heard in the ordinary course of events; only the reciter's voice is heard when the Quran is recited. However, it is possible for Allah to make His speech heard by suspending the usual laws, as happened when Musa heard it on Sinai, when the Prophet Muhammad heard it on the Night of Ascension, and as the believers will hear it in Paradise. This view maintains the transcendence of Allah's speech while allowing miraculous exceptions. Al-Baqillani is named as a proponent.

Al-Maturidi on Hearing Allah's Speech
Al-Maturidi's definitive position that only sounds are heard, not divine speech itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Maturidi held that Allah's speech cannot be heard directly and what the role of sound is in hearing divine speech.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is not heard; only a sound indicating it is heard.

This lesson presents the definitive position of Imam al-Maturidi on the nature of hearing divine speech. He argues that hearing in the created world is only connected to sounds, so it is impossible to hear something that is not a sound. Therefore, what was heard by prophets like Musa was a created sound indicating Allah's speech. This passage from the Tabṣirat al-Adilla clarifies the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the strong opening statement: 'The speech of Allah is not heard in any way whatsoever.'
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify al-Maturidi's main claim
The passage states that hearing is only attached to sounds. If something is not a sound, it cannot be heard. Thus, claiming one can hear Allah's speech directly is irrational.
This is the core logical argument.
3
Understand the reasoning
Al-Maturidi bases his view on the nature of hearing in this world (al-shahid). Hearing revolves around sound; without sound, there is no hearing. Hence, applying 'heard' to non-sound is a category error.
This is a principle of analogy (qiyas) from the witnessed to the unseen.
4
Examine the example of Musa
Al-Maturidi, in his Ta'wilat, explains that Musa heard a sound that indicated Allah's speech, not the speech itself. That sound was directly created by Allah as an honor, without human acquisition.
Other believers hear sounds acquired by human reciters, but Musa's sound was special.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many assume that 'hearing the Quran' means hearing Allah's speech itself. Al-Maturidi corrects this: we hear sounds that indicate the speech, not the eternal attribute.
This preserves divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : إن كلام الله لا يسمع بوجه من الوجوه، إذ يستحيل سماع ما ليس من جنس الحروف والأصوات إذ السماع في الشاهد يتعلق بالصوت ويدور معه وجودا وعدما ويستحيل إضافة كونه مسموعا إلى غير الصوت، فكان القول بجواز سماع ما ليس بصوت خروجا عن المعقول، وهو مذهب الشيخ أبي منصور الماتريدي نص عليه في كتاب التوحيد في آخر مسألة القرآن وقال : إن سماع الكلام ليس إلا سماع صوت دال عليه، وذكر فى كتاب التأويلات أن موسى عليه السلام سمع صوتا دالا على كلام الله تعالى، وكان اختصاصه أن الله تعالى أفهمه كلامه بإسماعه صوتا تولى تخليقه من غير أن يكون ذلك الصوت مكتسبا لأحد من الخلق، إكراما منه إياه، وغيره يسمعون صوتا مكتسبا للعباد فيفهمون به كلام الله تعالى ذكر نحو هذا. ذكرته لا بعبارته.
And among them are those who said: The speech of Allah is not heard in any way whatsoever, for it is impossible to hear what is not of the genus of letters and sounds, since hearing in the witnessed realm is attached to sound and revolves with it in existence and non-existence, and it is impossible to attribute being heard to other than sound. So the statement permitting hearing what is not a sound is a departure from reason. This is the doctrine of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, textually stated by him in the Book of Tawhid at the end of the chapter on the Quran, and he said: Hearing speech is nothing but hearing a sound that indicates it. And he mentioned in the Book of Ta'wilat that Musa, peace be upon him, heard a sound indicating the speech of Allah the Exalted, and his distinction was that Allah the Exalted made him understand His speech by making him hear a sound which He Himself created, without that sound being acquired by any of the creation, as an honor to him. Others hear a sound acquired by the servants and thereby understand the speech of Allah the Exalted. He mentioned something like this. I have reported it, not in his exact wording.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage gives the final position, that of al-Maturidi, which the author considers the correct one. It begins by stating unequivocally that Allah's speech is not heard at all. The reasoning is that hearing, in this world, is only connected to sounds; it is absurd to say something that is not a sound is heard. Therefore, claiming one can hear what is not a sound is irrational. Al-Maturidi's view is supported by his own writings: in his Kitab al-Tawhid, he states that hearing speech is merely hearing a sound that indicates it. In his Ta'wilat, he explains that what Musa heard was a created sound that indicated Allah's speech; the honor for Musa was that Allah Himself created that sound directly, without any human acquisition, whereas other people hear sounds acquired by human reciters and thereby understand Allah's speech. The author notes he is paraphrasing, not quoting verbatim.

Key Terms
كلام الله
سماع
صوت
الماتريدي
كتاب التوحيد
التأويلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hearing the recited Quran means hearing Allah's eternal speech directly.
Correction: Al-Maturidi clarifies that we only hear sounds that indicate the speech, not the speech itself. The speech remains uncreated and not directly heard.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, what did Musa actually hear on Mount Sinai?
A) The essence of Allah's speech.
B) A created sound indicating Allah's speech.
C) The voice of an angel.
D) Nothing at all.

Key Takeaway
For al-Maturidi, divine speech is never heard directly; only sounds that indicate it are heard, preserving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Quran recited, do you ever catch yourself thinking you are directly hearing Allah's speech? Does this lesson humble you to accept the ordinary means of hearing the Quran as sufficient? Commit to trusting the sound of the Quran as the honored vehicle for Allah's speech.

Two Views on Hearing the Speech of Allah
Nasafi reports two views on hearing kalam Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two classical positions on whether Allah's speech can be heard in this world: the denial by al-Qattan and the conditional affirmation by al-Baqillani.

Core Idea
Hearing Allah's speech is possible through divine intervention, beyond normal senses.

The question of whether humans can hear Allah's speech directly was debated among theologians. This passage from Nasafi presents two distinct positions: one that denies sense perception of accidents but allows hearing kalam Allah, and another that restricts normal hearing but permits miraculous hearing. You'll see how al-Baqillani uses examples from the prophets to defend the possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the contrast between the two views.
2
Understand al-Qattan's view
Identify why al-Qattan denies sense perception of accidents but allows hearing kalam Allah.
This is a subtle distinction.
3
Understand al-Baqillani's view
See how al-Baqillani distinguishes normal hearing from miraculous hearing.
Examples: Moses, Prophet's Ascension.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this relates to the nature of divine speech and the role of miracles.
This prepares for later lessons on kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يأبى وقوع الحس على شيء من الأعراض كما هو مذهب عبد الله بن سعيد القطان وجوزوا وقوع السمع على كلام الله تعالى. ومنهم من قال: إن كلام الله تعالى ليس بمسموع على العادة الجارية بل سمع صوت القارئ فحسب، ولكن من الجائز أن يسمع على قلب العادة الجارية كما موسى عليه السلام على الطور ومحمد الله ليلة المعراج على طريقة الكرامة، وقد يسمع المؤمنون ذلك أيضا في الآخرة. وإليه يذهب أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب الباقلاني من جملة الأشعرية.
It is denied that sense perception occurs upon any of the accidents, as is the doctrine of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, and they allowed hearing to occur upon the speech of Allah. And among them is one who said: Indeed, the speech of Allah, exalted is He, is not heard according to the customary practice, rather only the sound of the reciter is heard, but it is possible that it be heard by overturning the customary practice, just as Moses, peace be upon him, on the Mount, and Muhammad [؟] on the night of the Ascension in the manner of a miracle, and the believers will also hear that in the Hereafter. And to this goes Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Baqillani, among the Ash'aris.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage presents two positions on whether the speech of Allah (kalam Allah) can be heard. First, it mentions the view of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, who denies that sense perception (hiss) can occur upon accidents (a'rad). This is part of a broader discussion about whether attributes like speech, which are not material, can be perceived. The text then presents the view of al-Baqillani, who distinguishes between the usual way of hearing speech (through sounds) and a miraculous hearing. He states that normally only the sound of the reciter is heard, but it is possible for Allah's speech to be heard directly by breaking the customary practice (khariq li'l-ada), as happened with Moses on Mount Sinai and with the Prophet Muhammad on the night of the Ascension. This hearing is a miracle (karama). The believers will also hear Allah's speech in the Hereafter. Al-Baqillani is cited as the proponent of this view among the Ash'aris.

Key Terms
كلام الله (kalam Allah)
السماع (hearing)
الأعراض (accidents)
العادة الجارية (customary practice)
الكرامة (miracle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hearing Allah's speech is impossible in all circumstances.
Correction: Al-Baqillani allows it as a miracle, breaking customary practice. It is possible through divine intervention.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, how is the speech of Allah heard in this world?
A) Through natural sound
B) Not heard at all
C) By overturning customary practice
D) Only in dreams

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani allows hearing Allah's speech as a miracle, while al-Qattan has a different position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The lesson mentions that believers will hear Allah's speech in the Hereafter. Is that hope real in your heart? Does it affect how you listen to the Quran today? If your longing for that moment is weak, what needs to change in your heart so that you truly yearn to hear His speech?
