Al-Maturidi's View: Allah's Speech Is Not Heard
Nasafi presents al-Maturidi's argument that kalam Allah cannot be heard as sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's argument that Allah's speech is not heard as sound, and how he explains the case of Moses.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is not sound; hearing it requires an indicative sound.

Nasafi presents the distinct view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi that divine speech cannot be heard as letters and sounds. This argument relies on the principle that hearing in the physical world is always tied to sound. How then do prophets hear Allah? Al-Maturidi explains that they hear an indicative sound created by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Note the logical flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; focus on the reasoning first.
2
Identify the logical premise
Al-Maturidi argues that hearing in the witnessed world is only of sounds. Therefore, it is impossible to hear what is not sound. Find this premise in the text.
This premise is key to his entire argument.
3
Examine the Moses example
Al-Maturidi explains that Moses heard a sound indicative of Allah's speech, not the speech itself. Notice the distinction between Moses and others: Moses' sound was directly created by Allah, while others hear sounds acquired by humans.
This shows that even prophets do not hear the essence of divine speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : إن كلام الله لا يسمع بوجه من الوجوه، إذ يستحيل سماع ما ليس من جنس الحروف والأصوات إذ السماع في الشاهد يتعلق بالصوت ويدور معه وجودا وعدما ويستحيل إضافة كونه مسموعا إلى غير الصوت، فكان القول بجواز سماع ما ليس بصوت خروجا عن المعقول، وهو مذهب الشيخ أبي منصور الماتريدي نص عليه في كتاب التوحيد في آخر مسألة القرآن وقال : إن سماع الكلام ليس إلا سماع صوت دال عليه، وذكر فى كتاب التأويلات أن موسى عليه السلام سمع صوتا دالا على كلام الله تعالى، وكان اختصاصه أن الله تعالى أفهمه كلامه بإسماعه صوتا تولى تخليقه من غير أن يكون ذلك الصوت مكتسبا لأحد من الخلق، إكراما منه إياه، وغيره يسمعون صوتا مكتسبا للعباد فيفهمون به كلام الله تعالى ذكر نحو هذا. ذكرته لا بعبارته.
And among them is one who said: Indeed, the speech of Allah is not heard in any way, since it is impossible to hear what is not of the category of letters and sounds, because hearing in the witnessed world is related to sound and revolves with it in existence and non-existence, and it is impossible to attribute being heard to other than sound. Thus, the statement that it is permissible to hear what is not sound is a departure from what is rational. And this is the doctrine of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, which he stated in his book al-Tawhid at the end of the chapter on the Qur'an. He said: 'The hearing of speech is nothing but the hearing of a sound indicative of it.' And he mentioned in his book al-Ta'wilat that Moses, peace be upon him, heard a sound indicative of the speech of Allah. And his particularity was that Allah made him understand His speech by making him hear a sound whose creation He undertook, such that that sound was not acquired by any of the creation, as an honor from Him to him. Others hear sounds acquired by servants and thereby understand the speech of Allah. He mentioned something like this. I have related it, not in his own words.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who argues that Allah's speech (kalam Allah) cannot be heard in any way. His reasoning is that hearing in the physical world (shahid) is always attached to sound, and it is impossible for it to attach to something that is not sound. Therefore, claiming that one can hear what is not sound is irrational. Al-Maturidi refers to his book al-Tawhid where he states that hearing speech is nothing but hearing a sound that indicates it. In his Tafsir al-Ta'wilat, he explains that Moses heard a sound that indicated Allah's speech, and Allah gave Moses the understanding of that speech. The sound was specially created by Allah and not acquired by any servant, as an honor to Moses. Other people hear sounds acquired by human beings and thereby understand Allah's speech. The narrator (al-Nasafi) says he has related this meaning but not in al-Maturidi's exact words.

Correction: Al-Maturidi allows hearing an indicative sound that conveys the meaning of Allah's speech; he only denies hearing the speech as sound itself.

Ash'ari Agreement on Hearing Divine Speech
Al-Nasafi reports Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini's view and resolves the terminological dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the apparent disagreement among early Ash'ari mutakallimun about hearing divine speech is actually a difference in terminology, not in underlying doctrine.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris all agreed divine speech is heard via sound; their dispute was only about expression.

This passage from al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla reports the view of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and resolves a terminological dispute among early Ash'ari theologians. Al-Nasafi clarifies that while some said only sound is heard and others said meaning is known through sound, they all agreed on the same reality. This lesson explores that resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once through. Notice how al-Nasafi first reports the claim by Abu Ishaq and then explains the two positions.
Pay attention to the phrase 'fahadha minhum ikhtilaf fi al-tasmiyah la fi al-haqiqah'.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage describes two groups among the mutakallimun. One group (position 1) says only sound is heard. Another group (position 2) says that through hearing sound, one comes to know the meaning (speech in the soul) and the speaker's essence. Write down each position in your own words.
Both groups agree that it is impossible to hear what is not a sound.
3
Understand the resolution
Al-Nasafi concludes that the difference is only in naming (tasmiyah), not in reality (haqiqah). Reflect on why this distinction matters for theological discourse—how does it preserve unity while allowing different expressions?
This resolution shows that a disagreement in wording does not necessarily mean a disagreement in belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعده إلى هذا القول أبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني من جملة الأشعرية وذكر بعض الأشعرية أن أبا إسحاق أول من ذهب إلى هذا القول من متكلمى أهل الحديث. ولم يرض أبو إسحاق باختياره هذا المذهب حتى ادعى أن جميع من تقدمه من متكلمي أهل الحديث على هذا، واتفقوا على أن لا يمكن سماع ما ليس بصوت إلا أن اختلافهم في العبارة. فمنهم من اعتبر حقيقة السماع فقال : لا يسمع إلا الصوت، ومنهم من قال: لما سمع الصوت صار ما علم بعده ما دل عليه الصوت من الكلام القائم بالنفس وذات المتكلم معلوما، فلما صار ذلك معلوما بحاسة السمع بواسطة سماع الصوت كان عا[؟]، فإذا هذا منهم اختلاف في التسمية لا في الحقيقة. وذكر هذا في كتابه المسمى بترتيب المذهب. حكيته لا بلفظه.
And after him, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, from among the Ash'aris, went to this statement. And some Ash'aris mentioned that Abu Ishaq was the first to go to this statement among the mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith. And Abu Ishaq was not satisfied with his choice of this school until he claimed that all who preceded him from the mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith were upon this, and they agreed that it is not possible to hear what is not a sound, except that their difference is in expression. Among them is one who considered the reality of hearing and said: only sound is heard. And among them is one who said: when the sound is heard, what is known after it becomes known—that which the sound indicates of the speech subsisting in the soul and the essence of the speaker. So when that becomes known by the sense of hearing through the medium of hearing the sound, it was knowledge[?]. So this is from them a difference in naming, not in reality. And he mentioned this in his book called Tartib al-Madhhab. I reported it in meaning, not in his exact wording.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage reports and resolves a dispute among Ash'ari mutakallimun concerning the hearing of divine speech. Al-Nasafi states that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held the view that only sound can be heard, and that this view was also held by all earlier mutakallimun of ahl al-hadith. The apparent disagreement among them is only about phrasing: one group considers the literal meaning of hearing—so only sound is heard; another group says that through hearing the sound, one comes to know the meaning (the speech subsisting in the soul) and the essence of the speaker, so that knowledge is acquired by the sense of hearing via the sound. Al-Nasafi concludes that this is merely a difference in terminology, not in the underlying reality. He also notes that he reported the content of Abu Ishaq's book in meaning, not verbatim.

Key Terms
سماع
صوت
كلام قائم بالنفس
اختلاف في التسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'aris disagreed on whether divine speech is heard at all.
Correction: In fact, all Ash'aris agreed that divine speech is heard via sound; their disagreement was only about whether to call that 'hearing' directly or indirectly.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the real difference among the mutakallimun regarding hearing divine speech?
A) They disagreed on whether divine speech is created or uncreated.
B) They disagreed on whether speech subsisting in the soul can be heard directly.
C) Their difference is only in terminology, not in reality.
D) They disagreed on whether sound can convey meaning.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari mutakallimun all agreed that divine speech is heard via sound; the apparent dispute was merely terminological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a disagreement with another Muslim about a theological point, do you tend to focus on the exact words they use, or do you seek the underlying meaning they intend? How might this lesson on the Ash'aris resolving their terminological differences challenge you to be more charitable in your own disputes?
?
This passage shows that the Ash'ari scholars agreed on the core truth but expressed it differently. Are there areas in your own understanding of faith where you might be clinging to a particular expression while missing the shared reality? What would it look like to let go of your preferred wording for the sake of unity?
?
Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini claimed that all predecessors were upon his view. Reflect on a time you assumed others agreed with you only to find you were mistaken. How can you ensure you are not projecting your own understanding onto others when discussing matters of creed?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Divine Attribute of Takwin and Its Synonyms
Nasafi defines takwin and its synonymous terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that takwin and its five synonymous terms all refer to the same divine attribute: bringing the non-existent into existence, and why Nasafi specifies the term takwin.

Core Idea
All six creation terms point to one attribute: bringing existence from nothing.

This passage introduces a key divine attribute in Maturidi theology: takwin (bringing into existence). Nasafi clarifies that several Arabic terms often used for creation are actually synonyms, all referring to the same act of bringing the non-existent into existence. He then explains his preference for the term takwin, following the tradition of earlier scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Notice the six terms listed and the phrase ‘إخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود’. Then read the translation to confirm your understanding.
The passage is short, so read it slowly to absorb the technical vocabulary.
2
Identify the six synonymous terms
List the six terms: التكوين, التخليق, الخلق, الإيجاد, الإحداث, الاختراع. Understand that they are labeled ‘أسماء مترادفة’ (synonymous names).
All six are used in theological and Quranic contexts, but here they are treated as equivalent.
3
Grasp the single meaning
The common meaning is ‘إخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود’ – bringing the non-existent from non-existence into existence. This is the core definition of takwin as a divine attribute.
This act is unique to Allah; no creature can truly ‘bring into existence from nothing’.
4
Understand the specification of takwin
Nasafi says he specifies the word takwin to follow his predecessors. This reflects the Maturidi school’s tradition of using takwin as the technical term for the attribute of bringing into existence.
Other schools may use different terms (e.g., khalq), but the meaning remains the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن التكوين والتخليق والخلق والإيجاد والإحداث والاختراع، أسماء مترادفة يراد بها كلها معنى واحد وهو إخراج المعدوم من العدم إلى الوجود. فنخص استعمال لفظة التكوين اقتفاء لآثار أسلافنا - رحمهم الله في ذلك.
Know that takwin (bringing into existence), takhliq (creating), khalq (creation), ijad (bringing into being), ihdath (originating), and ikhtira' (inventing) are synonymous names, all of which intend one meaning: bringing the non-existent from non-existence into existence. So we specify the use of the word takwin, following in this the traces of our predecessors, may Allah have mercy on them.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'takwin' and its synonyms. It states that all these terms have a single meaning: bringing the non-existent into existence. It then explains that the author specifies the term 'takwin' following the practice of his predecessors.

Key Terms
التكوين
التخليق
الخلق
الإيجاد
الإحداث
الاختراع
المعدوم
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different terms (takwin, khalq, etc.) refer to different acts or attributes of Allah.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that these are synonymous names, all referring to the same act: bringing the non-existent into existence.

Checkpoint
What is the common meaning of the terms takwin, takhliq, khalq, ijad, ihdath, and ikhtira' according to Nasafi?
A) They all mean creating something from something.
B) They all mean bringing the non-existent into existence.
C) They all mean giving form to matter.
D) They all mean deceiving.

Key Takeaway
Takwin and its five synonyms all denote one divine attribute: bringing the non-existent from non-existence into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah created the heavens and the earth,' do you truly pause to imagine that they were absolutely nothing before? How does the reality of 'bringing from non-existence' change the way you look at the world around you? Commit this week to recite 'Allahu Khaliq' with full presence of heart, meditating on the nothingness from which everything came.
?
The six synonyms in this passage all point to one attribute. In your own life, do you sometimes fragment Allah's actions—thinking of His mercy as separate from His justice, His creation as separate from His provision? How can the unity of these terms teach you to see all of Allah's acts as flowing from one perfect attribute? Write down one area where you tend to separate Allah's attributes and seek to unify them in your understanding.
?
Nasafi followed his predecessors in using 'takwin' over other synonyms. Are you quick to adopt the terminology of the Salaf, or do you sometimes prefer your own wording? What does your choice of words reveal about your heart's attachment to the way of the early Muslims? Make a conscious effort this month to use the precise terms of the righteous predecessors when speaking about Allah's attributes.
